Is Witnessing Obligatory for Raj'ah?
Debates whether witnessing is obligatory or recommended for raj'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ishhād for raj'ah and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i/Maliki evidence.

Core Idea
An imperative does not always indicate obligation – context matters.

Does a husband need witnesses to take back his wife during the iddah? The Hanafi school says it is recommended, not required. This lesson unpacks the debate, focusing on the interpretation of Quranic imperatives and the consistency of each school's position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: ishhād is mandūb (recommended) upon raj'ah by word.
Mandūb means rewarded but not required.
2
Identify the opposing view
Malik and al-Shafi'i hold that ishhād is obligatory; without it, raj'ah is invalid.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
Examine the dalīl: absolute texts (imsāk, husband's right) and the Prophet's command without witnesses. The imperative in 65:2 is paired with fāriqūhunna (separation), which is not obligatory, so the command is for recommendation.
The Hanafis argue that requiring witnesses would be an addition tantamount to naskh.
4
Analyze the inconsistencies
The Hanafī response points out that Shafi'īs analogize to marriage yet do not require consent, dower, or guardian; Malik requires witnesses for raj'ah but not for marriage.
This shows the opposing view is inconsistent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafīs consider raj'ah valid without witnesses; Shāfi'īs/Mālikīs consider it invalid.
This difference matters for fatwa and practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإشهاد مندوب عليها [أي على الرجعة بالقول؛ لأن الإشهاد على الوطء لا يتحقق، قال محمد: لا تقبل الشهادة على التقبيل واللمس والنظر أنه بشهوة؛ لأنه لا علم للشاهد بها، وقال مالك والشافعي: لا تصح الرجعة إلا بالإشهاد؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَشْهِدُوا ذَوَيْ عَدْلٍ مِنْكُمْ (الطلاق:٢) أمر وهو للوجوب، ولنا: النصوص المطلقة كقوله تعالى: فَأَمْسِكُوهُنَّ (الطلاق:۲)، و﴿وَبُعُولَتُهُنَّ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَّ ﴾ (البقرة: ۲۲۸)، وكقوله عليه: 'مر ابنك فليراجعها' من غير قيد بالإشهاد واشتراطه زيادة، وهي نسخ، فلا يجوز إلا بمثله، والأمر في الآية محمول على الندب يدل عليه أنه قرنها بالمفارقة بقوله: ﴿ أَوْ فَارِقُوهُنَّ (الطلاق:٢)، وهي ليست شرطاً فيه، فكذا في الرجعة. والعجب منهم أنهم يشترطون الإشهاد في الرجعة اعتباراً بابتداء النكاح، ولا يشترطون رضاها ولا تجديد المهر ولا الولي، وأعجب منه أن مالكاً يشترط فيها الإشهاد، ولا يشترطه في ابتداء النكاح، وهذا الإشهاد مختص عندهم في الرجعة بالقول.
And witnessing is recommended (mandūb) upon it [i.e., upon raj'ah by word; because witnessing for intercourse cannot be established. Muhammad said: Testimony on kissing, touching, and looking with desire is not accepted because the witness has no knowledge of it. Malik and al-Shafi'i said: Raj'ah is not valid except with witnessing, due to the Almighty's statement: 'And call to witness two just persons among you' (65:2), an imperative which indicates obligation. Our evidence is the absolute texts like His statement: 'So retain them' (65:2) and 'And their husbands have more right to take them back' (2:228), and like his statement, peace be upon him: 'Order your son to take her back' without the condition of witnessing. Requiring witnessing is an addition, which amounts to abrogation (naskh), and that is not permissible except with similar evidence. The imperative in the verse is interpreted as recommendation (nadb); evidence for that is that He paired it with separation (fāriqūhunna), which is not a condition in it, so likewise in raj'ah. It is surprising that they require witnessing in raj'ah by analogy with the beginning of marriage, yet they do not require her consent, nor renewal of dower, nor a guardian. Even more surprising is that Malik requires witnessing in raj'ah but does not require it at the beginning of marriage. And this witnessing according to them is specifically for raj'ah by word.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage addresses whether witnessing (ishhād) is obligatory for raj'ah. The Hanafi position is that witnessing is mandūb (recommended), not obligatory. The evidence is that the Quranic verses about retaining wives (imsāk) are absolute and do not mention witnessing. The Prophet's command to a man to take back his wife also had no condition of witnesses. The imperative in 'Call to witness two just persons' is interpreted as recommendation, not obligation, because it is paired with 'or separate from them' (fāriqūhunna), and separation does not require witnesses. Therefore, the imperative is not for obligation. The Hanafis criticize the opposing view: if they analogize raj'ah to marriage (which requires witnesses), they should also require wife's consent, dower renewal, and guardian – which they do not. Malik's inconsistency is highlighted: he requires witnesses for raj'ah but not for initial marriage. The passage also notes that according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, testimony on actions like kissing is not accepted because the witness cannot be certain of desire.

Key Terms
الإشهاد (al-ishhād): The act of calling two just witnesses to attest to the raj'ah.
مندوب (mandūb): Recommended; an act whose performance is rewarded but omission not sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on witnessing (ishhād) for raj'ah?
A) Obligatory (wājib)
B) Recommended (mandūb)
C) Forbidden (harām)
D) Irrelevant

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ishhād for raj'ah is recommended, not obligatory, based on the pairing of the imperative with a non-obligatory action.

Conflict on Timing of Raj'ah: Hanafi vs Sahibayn
Resolves dispute when wife claims 'iddah expired and husband claims raj'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the validity of raj'ah when the wife immediately claims 'iddah expiry, and identify the practical consequence of each position.

Core Idea
Immediate wife claim of 'iddah expiry: Abu Hanifa says raj'ah void; Sahibayn say valid. Key: is 'iddah presumed ongoing until notification?

In this lesson, we examine a specific intra-Hanafi dispute on raj'ah. When a husband declares raj'ah and the wife instantly responds that her 'iddah has expired, do we consider the raj'ah valid? The answer splits Abu Hanifa from his two foremost students. We'll unpack their conflicting proofs and see how the disagreement shapes the final ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Disagreement
Understand the exact scenario: husband says 'I have taken you back' (raj'ah), and wife immediately says 'My 'iddah has expired.' Note the positions: Abu Hanifa says raj'ah is invalid; Sahibayn say it is valid.
The wife's response must be immediate (muttasil) for this dispute.
2
Analyze the Reasoning of Each Side
Sahibayn argue that the 'iddah is presumptively ongoing until notification; thus the raj'ah occurred while she was apparently still in 'iddah. Abu Hanifa argues that the wife is trustworthy (amīnah) about her own 'iddah; her immediate report necessarily implies that the 'iddah had already ended before the husband's statement.
The key difference: what weight to give to the wife's report versus the apparent continuity of 'iddah.
3
Determine the Practical Outcome (Thamarah)
The text then discusses a different scenario: if the wife remains silent and later claims expiry, she must take an oath; if she refuses, the raj'ah is valid. According to the commentary, this final ruling is according to the Sahibayn. Note how the ikhtilaf affects practical fatwa.
The text explicitly says 'lahumā' (according to the two) for the oath scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
راجعتك: أي لو قال لها: راجعتك، فقالت على الفور متصلا بقول الزوج: مضت عدتي، لم تصح الرجعة عنده، وقالا تصح؛ لأن الرجعة صادفت العدة؛ إذ هي باقية ظاهرا إلى أن تخبر هي انقضاؤها وقد سبق الرجعة إخبارها فتصح ولأبي حنيفة أنها صادفت حالة الانقضاء؛ لأنها أمينة في الإخبار عن الانقضاء، فإذا أخبرت مقارنا لقوله دل على سبق الانقضاء؛ إذ لا يمكنها الخبر إلا بعد الانقضاء، فعلم بالضرورة أن الانقضاء سابق على كلامهما؛ لأن صحة الخبر يقتضي سبق المخبر به بخلاف ما إذا سكتت ثم أخبرت بالانقضاء وعليها اليمين ههنا بالإجماع فإن نكلت صحت الرجعة. لهما. (فتح)
'Rāja'tuk': that is, if he said to her: 'I have taken you back,' and she immediately, connected to his speech, said: 'My 'iddah has expired,' then according to him (Abu Hanifa) the raj'ah is not valid, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is valid. This is because the raj'ah encountered the 'iddah; for it is apparently remaining until she informs of its expiry, and her informing preceded the raj'ah? Actually it is the other way: the text says 'preceded the raj'ah' but the meaning is that the expiry preceded? [There is an OCR confusion] Actually the text states: 'since it is remaining apparently until she informs of its expiry, and her informing preceded the raj'ah?' The correct reading: the two said it is valid because the raj'ah encountered the 'iddah, for it is apparently remaining until she informs of its expiry, and the raj'ah preceded her informing. For Abu Hanifa, it encountered the state of expiry; because she is trustworthy in reporting about expiry, so when she reports contemporaneously with his statement, it indicates that the expiry preceded; for she cannot report except after expiry, so it is known by necessity that the expiry preceded both of their statements; because the validity of a report necessitates that what is reported precedes. In contrast to when she remained silent and then reported expiry: in that case she would have to take an oath here by consensus, and if she refuses to take the oath, the raj'ah is valid. (According to the two, the oath is on her?) The text says: 'for the two, the oath is on her'? Actually it says 'lahumā' at the end, meaning the two hold that the oath is on her? Let's rely on the translation: The final part: 'and if she refuses, the raj'ah is valid, according to the two.'
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where the husband says 'I have taken you back' and the wife immediately responds 'My 'iddah has expired.' According to Abu Hanifa, the raj'ah is invalid because the wife's immediate report indicates that the 'iddah had already ended before the husband's statement. Since she is trustworthy regarding her own 'iddah, her near-simultaneous statement shows that the expiry preceded his raj'ah. Therefore, the raj'ah was made after the 'iddah, hence invalid. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that the raj'ah is valid because the 'iddah is presumed to continue until she actually reports its expiry. Since her report of expiry comes after (or simultaneously with) his announcement, the raj'ah took place while the 'iddah was still presumptively ongoing. The difference hinges on the presumption: Abu Hanifa gives more weight to the wife's immediate declaration, while the Sahibayn give weight to the apparent continuity of 'iddah until actual notification. The text also notes that if the wife remains silent and later claims expiry, he is not believed, and she must take an oath; if she refuses, the raj'ah is valid.

Key Terms
أمينة (amīnah): Trustworthy; the wife is considered trustworthy in reporting the expiry of her 'iddah.
متصل (muttasil): Immediately connected; the wife's response is immediate and not delayed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says 'I have taken you back' and the wife immediately says 'My 'iddah has expired', what is the ruling?
A) The raj'ah is valid
B) The raj'ah is invalid
C) The wife must take an oath
D) The husband's statement is ignored

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, the difference turns on whether the wife's immediate report is taken as proof that the 'iddah ended before the husband's action, or the 'iddah is presumed ongoing until notification.

Raj'ah Claim After Iddah: Wife's Confirmation and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on immediate denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a husband's claim of raj'ah after iddah and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf in the immediate-response scenario.

Core Idea
A husband's claim of raj'ah after iddah hinges on the wife's confirmation; if she denies, he is untrusted.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq deals with a practical scenario: a husband claims he performed raj'ah during the iddah, but only reports this after the iddah has ended. The ruling depends on whether the wife confirms or denies. The text then delves into a specific intra-Hanafi dispute when the wife immediately, upon hearing his claim, declares that her iddah has already ended. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principles of trustworthiness (amāna) and legal presumption (istishāb) within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
When a husband claims raj'ah after iddah, if the wife confirms his claim, the raj'ah is valid; if she denies, it is invalid. The husband is considered 'accused' (muttaham) because he reports a past act he cannot perform now.
2
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The dispute arises when the husband says 'I take you back' and the wife immediately responds 'My iddah has passed.' Abu Hanifa rules the raj'ah invalid; the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule it valid.
The disciples are often referred to as al-Sāḥibān.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Argument
Abu Hanifa argues that the woman is trustworthy (amīna) about her own iddah. Her immediate, connected reply proves that the iddah ended before the husband spoke, so the raj'ah encounters a state of termination, not the iddah period.
The principle: validity of a report requires the reported event to precede the report.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's Argument
The Sahibayn argue that the iddah remains apparent (ẓāhir) until the woman informs about its end. Since the husband performed raj'ah before she informed, it coincided with the iddah. Her subsequent report does not retroactively invalidate the raj'ah.
They rely on istishāb – the iddah is presumed ongoing until proven otherwise.
5
Note the Consensus Exception
If the wife remains silent and later reports the iddah's end, she must take an oath. If she refuses the oath, the raj'ah becomes valid by consensus. This shows the default position is validation unless the wife immediately speaks.
Consensus of the three Hanafi imams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال بعد العدة: راجعتك فيها فصدقته تصح، وإلا لا. وإلا لا أي وإن لم تصدق المرأة الزوج في قوله: كنت راجعتك في العدة، لا تصح الرجعة؛ لأنه أخبر عما لا يملك إنشاءه في الحال فكان متهما ولا مصدق له، ثم إذا لم يكن له برهان فلا يمين عليها عند أبي حنيفة. خلافاً راجعتك: أي لو قال لها: راجعتك، فقالت على الفور متصلا بقول الزوج: مضت عدتي، لم تصح الرجعة عنده، وقالا تصح؛ لأن الرجعة صادفت العدة؛ إذ هي باقية ظاهرا إلى أن تخبر هي. انقضاؤها وقد سبق الرجعة إخبارها فتصح ولأبي حنيفة أنها صادفت حالة الانقضاء؛ لأنها أمينة في الإخبار عن الانقضاء، فإذا أخبرت مقارنا لقوله دل على سبق الانقضاء؛ إذ لا يمكنها الخبر إلا بعد الانقضاء، فعلم بالضرورة أن الانقضاء سابق على كلامهما؛ لأن صحة الخبر يقتضي سبق المخبر به بخلاف ما إذا سكتت ثم أخبرت بالانقضاء وعليها اليمين ههنا بالإجماع فإن نكلت صحت الرجعة.
And if he says after the iddah: 'I took you back (rāja‘tuki) during it (the iddah),' and she confirms him, it is valid; otherwise not. 'Otherwise not' means if the woman does not confirm the husband in his saying 'I took you back during the iddah,' the raj'ah is not valid because he reported something he cannot create now, so he is accused and not believed. Then if he has no proof, no oath is upon her according to Abu Hanifa. As for the case 'rāja‘tuki': if he says to her 'I took you back' and she immediately, connected to his speech, says 'my iddah has passed,' the raj'ah is not valid according to him (Abu Hanifa), but the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is valid because the raj'ah encountered the iddah; it still remains apparently until she informs. Its end preceded the raj'ah? Actually she informed, so it is valid. According to Abu Hanifa, it encountered the state of termination because she is trustworthy in reporting the end. When she reports concurrently with his speech, it indicates that the termination preceded; because she cannot report except after termination. So it is known by necessity that the termination preceded both their statements, because the validity of the report requires that the reported matter precede it. Unlike if she remained silent and then reported the termination, and she has an oath here by consensus; if she refuses to swear, the raj'ah is valid.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where a husband claims he performed raj'ah during the iddah period, but he makes this claim after the iddah has already ended. The ruling is given: if the wife confirms his claim, the raj'ah is valid; if she denies it, it is not valid. The reason for the invalidity upon her denial is that the husband is reporting a past act that he cannot currently perform; thus he is considered untrustworthy and is not believed without evidence. If he has no proof, Abu Hanifa rules that no oath is imposed on her. The passage then addresses a specific sub-case: what if the husband says 'rāja‘tuki' (I take you back) and the wife immediately, in direct connection to his statement, says 'maddat iddati' (my iddah has passed)? Here there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the raj'ah is not valid because the wife's statement, since it is concurrent with his, shows that the iddah had already ended before he spoke. Her report is trustworthy, and the validity of a report requires that the thing reported (the end of iddah) preceded the act of reporting. Therefore, the raj'ah occurred after the iddah, making it invalid. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that it is valid because the iddah is still considered ongoing in apparent terms until the woman reveals its end. Since he performed raj'ah before she informed about the end, it coincides with the iddah period. However, Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that since she is trustworthy about her own iddah, her immediate response proves that the iddah ended before his utterance, so the raj'ah is invalid. If she remained silent and later reported the end, there is an oath upon her by consensus; if she refuses to take the oath, the raj'ah becomes valid because she is not confirming the end.

Key Terms
تصح (tasihhu): The raj'ah is legally valid and effective.
متهما (muttahaman): Accused; the husband is not trusted because he claims a past act he cannot prove.
أمينة (amīna): Trustworthy; the woman is trusted about her own iddah because she knows it best.
خلافاً (khilāfan): In opposition to; signaling a divergent view.
اليمين (al-yamīn): The oath; here, the wife may be required to swear that the iddah ended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a husband says 'rāja‘tuki' and the wife immediately says 'maddat iddati', what is the ruling?
A) Raj'ah is valid because the iddah is still apparent.
B) Raj'ah is invalid because the iddah had already ended.
C) Raj'ah is valid only if the wife takes an oath.
D) Raj'ah is valid and the wife must confirm later.

Key Takeaway
A husband's claim of raj'ah after iddah is only valid if the wife confirms; if she immediately denies, Abu Hanifa invalidates it, while the Sahibayn uphold it based on the apparent continuity of iddah.

Raj'ah of a Slave-Girl: Whose Word Prevails?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on credibility in slave-girl's raj'ah claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whose statement is accepted in a slave-girl's raj'ah dispute and explain the conflicting proofs (talil).

Core Idea
In disputes over a slave-girl's iddah, her word is believed when it concerns her body, but the master's word prevails regarding his property right.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on a specific scenario: after the iddah has expired, the husband claims he took back his wife (raj'ah) during the iddah, the master confirms, but the slave-girl denies. Whose statement is taken? The same passage also presents an agreed case where the slave-girl's word is accepted. We will walk through the proofs (talil) and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The husband claims raj'ah occurred during iddah; the master confirms; the slave-girl denies. Determine whose statement should be accepted.
2
State the core principle: raj'ah depends on iddah
Raj'ah is only valid if the iddah is ongoing. Therefore, the existence of iddah is crucial.
Abu Hanifa uses this dependency to argue that the person who is believed about iddah should also be believed about what depends on it.
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Since a woman is authoritative about her own iddah (due to knowledge of her body), she should also be authoritative about raj'ah which is built upon it.
Abu Hanifa gives the statement to the slave-girl.
4
Follow the Sahibayn's reasoning
The sexual right (bud') of the slave-girl is the master's property. When it comes to ownership, the owner's statement is preferred.
The Sahibayn give the statement to the master.
5
Distinguish the second case (agreed)
When the slave-girl states her iddah has ended and the master and husband deny, her statement is accepted by all because she is most knowledgeable of her own condition and trustworthy.
This case is agreed upon, showing that personal knowledge overrides property interest when it concerns the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال زوج الأمة بعد العدة: راجعتك فيها، وصدقه سيدها وكذبته الأمة فالقول لها عند الإمام؛ لأن الرجعة تبتني على قيام العدة والقول فيها قولها فكذا فيما يبتني عليها. وعندهما القول قول المولى؛ لأن البضع ملكه وهو خالص حقه. وأما الثانية فيما إذا قالت الأمة: انقضت عدتي، وأنكرها الزوج والسيد فالقول قولها، وهذه المسألة اتفاقية؛ لأنه أعرف بحالها وهي أمينة فيه، فيقبل قولها دون المولى والزوج.
And if the husband of a slave-girl says after the iddah: 'I took you back during it,' and her master confirms him but the slave-girl denies him, then the statement (al-qawl) is hers according to Imam Abu Hanifa; because raj'ah is based on the existence of iddah, and the statement about iddah is hers, so similarly regarding what is based on it. According to the two disciples, the statement is that of the master because the pudendum (al-bud') is his property and is exclusively his right. As for the second case, when the slave-girl says: 'My iddah ended,' and both the husband and master deny it, then the statement is hers, and this matter is agreed upon (ittifāqiyyah) because she is more aware of her own state and is trustworthy in it, so her statement is accepted without that of the master and husband.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage addresses two scenarios involving a slave-girl (amah) who is married and then divorced revocably. In the first scenario, the husband claims after the iddah that he performed raj'ah during it. The master (sayyid) confirms the husband's claim, but the slave-girl herself denies it. Here, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the slave-girl's statement (qawl) is accepted over the master's, because the validity of raj'ah depends on the existence of the iddah, and the woman's statement about her iddah is authoritative. Since raj'ah is built upon that, her word should prevail. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) rule that the master's statement is accepted, because the sexual right (bud') of the slave-girl belongs to the master, and this right is exclusively his. He has a direct interest in whether raj'ah occurred. In the second scenario, the slave-girl states that her iddah has ended, and both the husband and the master deny it. In this case, there is agreement (ittifāq) that the slave-girl's statement is accepted over both the husband and the master. The reason given is that she is more knowledgeable about her own bodily condition and is considered trustworthy (amīna) in this matter. So her statement is accepted, and the denial of the husband and master does not affect it.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave who is married.
البضع (al-bud'): The sexual right or ownership of the slave-girl's body; her master owns it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the husband of a slave-girl claims raj'ah during her iddah after it ended, and the master confirms but the slave-girl denies, whose statement is accepted according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The husband's statement
B) The slave-girl's statement
C) The master's statement
D) An independent witness

Key Takeaway
In disputes over a slave-girl's iddah, her personal knowledge of her body prevails in matters directly about her condition, but property rights may override in related matters per Sahibayn.

When Raj'ah is Cut Off: End of Iddah by Menstrual Purity
Ruling on when the husband's right to raj'ah ends, with details on pure blood and ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the husband's right to raj'ah ends based on the wife's menstrual purity, and apply the conditions for ten-day vs less-than-ten-day purity.

Core Idea
For ten days of purity, raj'ah ends immediately; for less, it requires ghusl or prayer time passage.

In Hanafi law, the husband's right to take back his wife during the revocable iddah (raj'ah) ends when the iddah itself ends. For a woman whose iddah is measured by menstruation, the end of her third period marks the limit. But the exact moment depends on how long her purity lasts. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the conditions clearly, including disputes and exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stage of iddah
The woman is in her third menstruation after a revocable divorce. The iddah ends when she becomes pure (tāhir) from this menstruation.
For a free woman, iddah is three menstrual cycles; for a slave, two. The passage assumes a free woman.
2
Distinguish between ten-day purity and less
If the pure state lasts a full ten days (the maximum menstrual period), raj'ah is cut off immediately upon the cessation of blood, without needing ghusl. For less than ten days, raj'ah is not cut off until she either performs ghusl or a full prayer time passes.
The ten-day rule is based on menstrual maximum; less than ten days leaves possibility of return of blood, so confirmation is needed.
3
Understand the role of ghusl and prayer time
For less-than-ten-day purity, ghusl confirms purity. If she cannot or does not perform ghusl, then the passage of one complete prayer time (waqt al-salāh) serves as confirmation because she is now required to pray, thus treated as pure.
This applies to Muslim women. Kitabiyyah and insane women are exceptions.
4
Analyze the tayammum case
If she performs tayammum (dry ablution due to lack of water) and prays, raj'ah is cut off upon finishing the prayer (according to the correct view). If she only does tayammum without praying, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: istihsān keeps raj'ah, qiyās cuts it off.
The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold istihsān; Imam Muhammad alone holds qiyās, but the muftā bihi is the view of the two.
5
Apply exceptions for kitabiyyah and insane women
For a Jewish or Christian woman (kitābiyyah) or a woman who is insane or mentally disabled, raj'ah is cut off even if purity lasts less than ten days, because they are not obligated to perform ghusl or pray.
The text says 'madhnūnah wa ma'tūhah' – the insane and the mentally deficient – are like the kitābiyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتنقطع إن طهرت من الحيض الأخير لعشرة أيام وإن لم تغتسل، ولأقل لا حتى تغتسل أو يمضي وقت صلاة، أو تيممت وتصلي. أي إذا انقطع دم الحيض الأخير لعشرة أيام انقطعت الرجعة وإن لم تغتسل؛ لأن الحيض لا مزيد له على العشرة فبمجرد الانقطاع خرجت من الحيض فانقضت العدة وانقطعت الرجعة، وقال زفر بحظيه: لا تنقطع الرجعة ما لم تغتسل اعتباراً بما إذا انقطع الدم لأقل منها، وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولنا: أنها لما خرجت من الحيضة الثالثة، فقد انقضت العدة. ولأقل لا أي وإن طهرت من هذا الحيض لأقل من عشرة أيام لم ينقطع الرجعة حتى تغتسل أو يمضي عليها وقت صلاة؛ لأنه يحتمل عود الدم، فلابد أن يتأكد الانقطاع بحقيقة الاغتسال أو بلزوم حكم من أحكام الطاهرات، وذلك بمضي وقت صلاة؛ لأنها تطالب بالقضاء، فالتحقت بالطاهرات وهذا إذا كانت مسلمة، والكتابية تنقطع رجعتها بمجرد الانقطاع بما دون العشرة؛ لعدم خطابها للغسل والصلاة، وينبغي أن تكون المجنونة والمعتوهة كذلك. أو تيممت وتصلي مطلقا سواء كانت مكتوبة أو تطوعاً، ثم قيل: ينقطع بنفس الشروع عند الشيخين والصحيح أن الرجعة لا تنقطع عندهما ما لم تفرغ من الصلاة، وإن تيممت ولم تصل بقي حق الرجعة استحسانا وهو قولهما، وعند محمد الله: لم يبق وهو القياس؛ لأن التيمم حال عدم الماء ينزل منزلة الاغتسال بدليل جواز الصلاة وجواز دخول المسجد وغيره من الأحكام.
And it (the right of raj'ah) is cut off if she becomes pure (tahirat) from the last menstruation for ten days even if she does not perform ghusl. For less than that, it is not cut off until she performs ghusl or a prayer time passes, or she performs tayammum and prays. That is: if the blood of the last menstruation stops for ten days, the raj'ah is cut off even without ghusl; because menstruation does not exceed ten days, so by mere cessation she exits menstruation, the iddah ends, and raj'ah is cut off. And Zufar and his two companions said: raj'ah is not cut off until she performs ghusl, analogizing to the case where blood stops for less than ten. And according to us, since she has exited the third menstruation, the iddah has ended. For less than ten: even if she becomes pure from this menstruation for less than ten days, raj'ah is not cut off until she performs ghusl or a prayer time passes over her; because blood may return, so the cessation must be confirmed by actual ghusl or by the application of a ruling of the pure women, which is the passing of a prayer time; because she is required to make up the prayer, so she joins the pure women. This applies if she is Muslim. A kitābiyyah (Scripture woman) has her raj'ah cut off by mere cessation even for less than ten, because she is not addressed for ghusl and prayer. The insane and mentally disabled are likewise. Or she performs tayammum and prays, absolutely, whether the prayer is obligatory or supererogatory. Then it is said: it is cut off by the very act of starting (the prayer) according to the two shaykhs; but the correct view is that raj'ah is not cut off according to them until she finishes the prayer. If she performs tayammum but does not pray, the right of raj'ah remains according to istihsān, and this is the view of the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (al-Shaybānī), it does not remain, and this is qiyās; because tayammum in the absence of water takes the place of ghusl, as evidenced by the permissibility of prayer, entering the mosque, and other rulings. There is no difference between the ruling that a prayer is valid and the ruling that it is permissible to initiate it; each requires purity.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage explains when the husband's right to raj'ah is cut off after a revocable divorce. It focuses on the end of the iddah through menstruation. For a free woman, the iddah ends after three complete menstrual cycles (or three months if no menstruation). The cutting off of raj'ah occurs when she becomes pure (tāhir) from the third menstruation. There is a condition regarding the duration of purity. If she is pure for ten full days (the maximum length of a menstrual period), then raj'ah is cut off immediately upon the cessation of blood, even if she has not yet performed ghusl (ritual bath). This is because menstruation cannot exceed ten days, so the mere stop confirms the end of menstruation. Zufar and his followers (the 'three'?) disagreed, saying raj'ah remains until she performs ghusl, analogizing to the case where purity lasts less than ten days. The Hanafi response is that since she has exited the third menstruation, the iddah is complete. If the purity lasts less than ten days (e.g., she sees blood stop for only a few days), raj'ah is not cut off until she either performs ghusl or a full prayer time (waqt al-salāh) passes. This is because the blood may return, so the cessation must be confirmed by actual ghusl or by a ruling that treats her as pure, such as the obligation to pray. If she performs tayammum and prays (due to no water), then there is a dispute: some say raj'ah is cut off by starting the prayer, but the correct view (sahīh) is that it is cut off only upon finishing the prayer. If she only performs tayammum without praying, the right remains according to istihsān (the view of the two disciples), but according to Imam Muhammad, it does not remain (qiyās). The text also notes exceptions: for a kitābiyyah (Jewish or Christian woman), raj'ah is cut off by mere cessation even for less than ten days because she is not obligated to perform ghusl or pray. Similarly for the insane and mentally disabled.

Key Terms
الطهر (at-tuhr): Purity; the cessation of menstrual blood.
الاغتسال (al-ightisāl): The ritual bath to remove major impurity.
التيمم (at-tayammum): Dry ablution with earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable.
الرجعة (ar-raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his wife during the revocable iddah.
الكتابية (al-kitābiyyah): A Jewish or Christian woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when a woman becomes pure from her third menstruation for ten days, when does the husband's right to raj'ah end?
A) After she performs ghusl
B) Immediately upon the blood stopping, even without ghusl
C) After she prays one prayer
D) After a full day of purity

Key Takeaway
The end of raj'ah depends on the duration of menstrual purity: ten days ends it immediately; less requires confirmation by ghusl or prayer time passage.

Dispute on Slave Woman's Raj'ah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony in slave woman's raj'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whose testimony is accepted in a raj'ah dispute involving a slave woman and identify the legal basis for each position.

Core Idea
When a slave woman denies raj'ah but her master confirms, Imam trusts her word; the Sahibayn trust the master — a clash between personal knowledge and property rights.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on whose testimony is accepted when a slave woman's raj'ah is contested. The key factor is whether the woman's knowledge of her own 'iddah outweighs the master's ownership of her vulva. The dispute reveals how different legal presumptions — amānāt versus property rights — lead to divergent rulings within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases. Name them and note which scenario is disputed and which is agreed upon.
Scenario 1: Husband claims raj'ah during 'iddah, master confirms, woman denies. Scenario 2: Woman claims 'iddah ended, husband and master deny.
2
Locate the point of disagreement
In Scenario 1, who disagrees with whom? What exactly is the issue being disputed?
Imam Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whose statement is accepted.
3
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's dalil
Why does Abu Hanifa prefer the slave woman's statement? What principle or analogy does he use?
His reasoning: raj'ah is based on the existence of 'iddah, and the woman's word is accepted about her 'iddah; so too for what is based on it.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's dalil
Why do the Sahibayn prefer the master's statement? What legal principle do they invoke?
Their reasoning: the vulva (al-bid') is the master's property, and he has exclusive right over it; his word on his property prevails.
5
Note the agreed scenario
In Scenario 2, all imams agree. What is the reasoning for this unanimity?
The woman knows her own body best and is trustworthy regarding her 'iddah; her word overrides both the master and husband.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرجعة مضت عدتي. وإن قال زوج الأمة بعد العدة: راجعتك فيها، وصدقه سيدها أي بعد انقضاء العدة ك س م. أي في العدة الزوج مولى الأمة وكذبته، أو قالت مضت عدّتي وأنكرا فالقول لها. وتنقطع إن طهرت من الحيض أي الأمة الزوج الأمة أي الزوج والمولى أي للأمة في المسألتين أي الرجعة أي المطلقة الأخير لعشرة أيام وإن لم تغتسل، ولأقل لا حتى تغتسل أو يمضي وقت صلاة أو تيممت أي لا تنقطع الرجعة عليها أدنى مضت عدتي: فإنه لا تصح الرجعة اتفاقاً إذا قالت على الفور مقارناً بقوله (ط) فالقول لها: أي في الصورتين، أما الأولى يعني إذا قال زوج الأمة بعد انقضاء عدتها قد كنت راجعتها في العدة، وصدقه سيد الأمة وكذبته الأمة فالقول قولها عند الإمام؛ لأن الرجعة تبتني على قيام العدة والقول فيها ،قولها فكذا فيما يبتني عليها، وعندهما القول قول المولى؛ لأن البضع ملكه وهو خالص حقه. وأما الثانية أي فيما إذا قالت الأمة: انقضت عدتي، وأنكرها الزوج والسيد فالقول قولها، وهذه المسألة اتفاقية؛ لأنه أعرف بحالها وهي أمينة فيه، فيقبل قولها دون المولى والزوج
Chapter of Raj'ah. My 'iddah has passed. And if the husband of the slave woman says after the 'iddah: 'I have taken you back in it,' and her master confirms him (i.e., after the completion of the 'iddah), meaning during the 'iddah the husband and the master (i.e., the master of the slave woman) [agree], and she denies, or she says: 'My 'iddah has passed' and they both deny, then the statement is hers. And it is cut off if she becomes pure from the last menstruation (i.e., the slave woman, the husband, the slave woman, i.e., the husband and the master) for ten days even if she hasn't performed ghusl, and for less than that no, until she performs ghusl or the time of prayer passes, or she performs tayammum (i.e., the raj'ah is not cut off). Her lowest [?] 'my 'iddah has passed': indeed, the raj'ah is not valid by agreement if she says it immediately corresponding to his statement. (T) So the statement is hers: i.e., in both situations. As for the first, meaning if the husband of the slave woman after the completion of her 'iddah says 'I had taken you back during the 'iddah,' and the master of the slave woman confirms him and the slave woman denies, then the statement is hers according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because raj'ah is based on the existence of the 'iddah and the statement in it is hers, so likewise in what is based on it. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the statement is the master's, because the vulva is his property and it is his exclusive right. As for the second, i.e., when the slave woman says: 'My 'iddah has passed,' and the husband and the master deny, then the statement is hers. This issue is agreed upon, because she is more knowledgeable about her own state and she is trustworthy in it, so her statement is accepted over the master and the husband.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the credibility of statements in matters of raj'ah involving a slave woman. The text presents two scenarios. First: the husband claims he performed raj'ah during the 'iddah, the master confirms, but the slave woman denies. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, her statement is accepted because raj'ah depends on the existence of the 'iddah, and she is the one whose word is accepted about her own 'iddah. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the master's statement is accepted because he owns her vulva and it is his exclusive right. Second: both the husband and the master deny the slave woman's claim that her 'iddah has passed; here all agree her statement is accepted because she knows her own state and is trustworthy. The passage also briefly mentions the ruling on cutting off raj'ah when she becomes pure from the last menstruation, but this is introductory to the next section and not part of the dispute.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) during the wife's 'iddah, by which the marriage is restored without a new contract.
أمة (amah): A female slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave woman's husband claims he performed raj'ah during her 'iddah, and her master confirms him but she denies, whose statement is accepted according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The husband's statement
B) The master's statement
C) The slave woman's statement
D) Both the master and husband's statement

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has a legitimate internal ikhtilaf on whether the slave woman's or master's testimony prevails in raj'ah disputes, reflecting a deeper tension between personal knowledge and property rights.

Cutting off Raj'ah at Ten-Day Pure Interval
Ruling with proof on when menstruation cessation ends raj'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when raj'ah ends after the third menstruation and distinguish the two scenarios: cessation for ten full days vs. less than ten.

Core Idea
A full ten-day cessation ends raj'ah immediately; less than ten requires ghusl or a prayer time to pass.

The right of raj'ah (return to marriage) ends when the wife's 'iddah is complete. This passage clarifies the precise moment at which the 'iddah ends when it is based on menstruation. It distinguishes between a full ten-day stoppage and a shorter one, explains the reasoning, and addresses special cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First: the third menstruation ceases for ten days. Second: it ceases for less than ten days. Each has a different ruling.
The ten-day period is the maximum for menstruation in Hanafi fiqh.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
At ten days, raj'ah ends immediately upon cessation, even without ghusl. For less than ten, raj'ah continues until she performs ghusl or the time of a prayer passes.
The proof is that ten days is the maximum; after that, the blood is not menstrual, so the 'iddah is complete.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar argues that even at ten days, raj'ah does not end until ghusl, by analogy with the less-than-ten case. The Hanafi position (lana) is that mere cessation suffices because the maximum has been reached.
The text prefers the Hanafi view, which is mufta bihi.
4
Apply to non-Muslim and insane women
For a Kitabiyya (Jewish or Christian) woman, the 'iddah ends immediately even for less-than-ten cessation because she is not obligated to ghusl or prayer. The same applies to an insane or mentally deficient woman.
This is based on the absence of the obligation that confirms purity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتنقطع إن طهرت من الحيض الأخير لعشرة أيام وإن لم تغتسل، ولأقل لا حتى تغتسل أو يمضي وقت صلاة. (ط) لعشرة أيام علة لـ "طهرت" أي لأجل تمامها سواء انقطع الدم أو لا. (نهر) أي إذا انقطع دم الحيض الأخير لعشرة أيام انقطعت الرجعة وإن لم تغتسل؛ لأن الحيض لا مزيد له على العشرة فبمجرد الانقطاع خرجت من الحيض فانقضت العدة وانقطعت الرجعة، وقال زفر بحظيه: لا تنقطع الرجعة ما لم تغتسل اعتباراً بما إذا انقطع الدم لأقل منها، وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولنا: أنها لما خرجت من الحيضة الثالثة، فقد انقضت العدة. (رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٧/١) (مستخلص) ولأقل لا [ أي وإن طهرت من هذا الحيض لأقل من عشرة أيام (ط) أي وانقطع دم الحيض الأخير لأقل من عشرة أيام لم ينقطع الرجعة حتى تغتسل أو يمضي عليها وقت صلاة؛ لأنه يحتمل عود الدم، فلابد أن يتأكد الانقطاع بحقيقة الاغتسال أو بلزوم حكم من أحكام الطاهرات، وذلك بمضي وقت صلاة؛ لأنها تطالب بالقضاء، فالتحقت بالطاهرات وهذا إذا كانت مسلمة، والكتابية تنقطع رجعتها بمجرد الانقطاع بما دون العشرة؛ لعدم خطابها للغسل والصلاة، وينبغي أن تكون المجنونة والمعتوهة كذلك.
And it is cut off if she becomes pure from the last menstruation for ten days even if she hasn't performed ghusl, and for less than ten no, until she performs ghusl or the time of prayer passes. (T) 'For ten days' is the cause for 'she became pure' i.e., for its completion, whether the blood stopped or not. (Nahr) i.e., if the blood of the last menstruation stops for ten days, the raj'ah is cut off even if she hasn't performed ghusl, because menstruation has no increase beyond ten, so by mere cessation she exits menstruation, her 'iddah expires, and raj'ah is cut off. Zufar said in his opinion: raj'ah is not cut off until she performs ghusl, analogously to when the blood stops for less than ten, and the three said the same. Our argument: when she has exited the third menstruation, the 'iddah has expired. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/267) (Mukhtasar) And for less than ten no [ i.e., if she becomes pure from this menstruation for less than ten days (T) i.e., if the blood of the last menstruation stops for less than ten days, raj'ah is not cut off until she performs ghusl or the time of prayer passes, because blood may return, so the cessation must be confirmed by actual ghusl or by the application of a ruling of the pure, and that is by the passing of a prayer time, because she is required to make up the prayer, so she joins the ranks of the pure. This is if she is Muslim. The Kitabiyya's raj'ah is cut off by mere cessation for less than ten, because she is not addressed with ghusl and prayer. The insane and the mentally deficient should be the same.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the point at which the husband's right of raj'ah ends when the wife completes her 'iddah by menstruation. It covers two cases. First: if the last menstrual bleeding stops after a full ten days (the maximum menstrual period), the 'iddah ends immediately upon cessation, even if she has not performed ghusl, because menstruation cannot exceed ten days. Therefore raj'ah is cut off. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagreed, saying raj'ah continues until ghusl, by analogy with the less-than-ten case. The 'three' (likely the three other schools or the three companions) agreed with Zufar. The Hanafi position (represented by 'lana') is that exiting the third menstruation completes the 'iddah. Second: if the bleeding stops for less than ten days, raj'ah continues until she performs ghusl or until the time of a full prayer passes, because the cessation might be temporary and blood may return. The passing of a prayer time confirms the cessation since she is required to pray. For a Kitabiyya (Jewish/Christian woman), raj'ah ends immediately upon cessation for less than ten days because she is not obligated to perform ghusl or prayer. Similarly for an insane or mentally deficient woman.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying; for a divorced free woman, it is three menstrual cycles.
كتابية (kitabiyya): A Jewish or Christian woman (People of the Book) who is married to a Muslim man.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim woman's third menstruation stops after five days, when does her right of raj'ah end?
A) Immediately upon cessation
B) After she performs ghusl or a prayer time passes
C) After ten days from cessation
D) Only after she performs ghusl and prays

Key Takeaway
The right of raj'ah ends immediately when the third menstruation ceases for ten full days; for shorter cessations, it requires confirmation through ghusl or the passing of a prayer time.

Tayammum and Ending Raj'ah: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tayammum and raj'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether tayammum ends raj'ah and explain the reasoning of the two shaykhs versus Muhammad ibn al-Hasan.

Core Idea
Tayammum ends raj'ah only after completing a full prayer — the two shaykhs overrule strict qiyas by istihsan.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement about the effect of tayammum on the marital bond during the iddah period of a revocable divorce (raj'ah). The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents two opposing views from the founding imams of the school, each supported by a different method of reasoning: qiyas and istihsan. Understanding how the later preference (tarjih) is established will deepen your appreciation of Hanafi legal methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The ruling concerns a woman whose menstruation stops for less than ten days, she cannot find water, and she performs tayammum. The question is whether this tayammum, with or without prayer, ends the husband's right to take her back (raj'ah).
The scenario is specific to a woman in her iddah after a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i).
2
State the two intra-Hanafi positions
The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that raj'ah is not cut off by tayammum alone; it ends only after she finishes a complete prayer (obligatory or voluntary) with that tayammum. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan holds that tayammum alone cuts off raj'ah immediately, even without prayer.
Muhammad's view is supported by Zufar and the three (other schools – Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali).
3
Analyze the proof of each side
Muhammad uses qiyas: tayammum is like ghusl in permitting acts that require purification, so it should also end raj'ah like ghusl does. The two shaykhs use istihsan: tayammum is a necessary purification (darurah) that does not remove impurity with certainty; its effects are limited to the necessity, so it does not extend to ending raj'ah unless completed by prayer.
The key difference is the nature of tayammum: perfect purification vs. concession.
4
Identify the relied-upon view (mufta bihi)
The passage explicitly states that 'the correct view is that raj'ah is not cut off until she finishes the prayer.' This indicates preference for the two shaykhs' position. Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Marghinani, al-Haskafi) uphold this as the operative ruling.
Tarjih is based on the principle that what is established by necessity is limited to its extent.
5
Apply the dispute to a practical case
If a woman in iddah performs tayammum but does not pray, the husband retains the right to take her back according to the mufta bihi view. If she prays a complete prayer with that tayammum, the right ends.
If she prays but later finds water, the prayer is valid, but raj'ah has already been cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو تيممت وتصلي [ أن لم تقدر على الماء بعد ما طهرت لدون عشرة. (ط)] مطلقا سواء كانت مكتوبة أو تطوعاً، ثم قيل: ينقطع بنفس الشروع عند الشيخين والصحيح أن الرجعة لا تنقطع عندهما ما لم تفرغ من الصلاة، وإن تيممت و لم تصل بقي حق الرجعة استحسانا وهو قولهما، وعند محمد الله: لم يبق وهو القياس؛ لأن التيمم حال عدم الماء ينزل منزلة الاغتسال بدليل جواز الصلاة وجواز دخول المسجد وغيره من الأحكام، ولا فرق بين الحكم بجواز صلاة أديت وبين الحكم بجواز الإقدام على أدائها؛ إذ كل واحد منهما يشترط له الطهارة، فإذا كان كالاغتسال في حق الأحكام، فكذا في حق هذا الحكم، بل أولى؛ لأن انقطاع الرجعة يؤخذ فيه بالاحتياط، وبه قال زفر له والثلاثة، ولهما: أن التيمم طهارة ضرورية؛ لكونها تلويثاً حقيقة؛ لأنه لا يرفع الحدث بيقين حتى لو وجد الماء كان محدثاً بالحدث السابق، وإنما جعل طهارة حكماً ضرورة الحاجة إلى أداء الصلاة؛ كي لا تتضاعف عليها الواجبات والثابت ضرورة يتقدر بقدرها. (عيني، فتح) وتصلي بالتيمم فرضاً أو نفلاً صلاة تامة في الأصح. (ع)
Or she performs tayammum and prays [ after she became pure for less than ten and cannot find water. (T)] absolutely, whether it is obligatory or voluntary. Then it is said: it is cut off by the mere commencement according to the two shaykhs, but the correct view is that raj'ah is not cut off according to them until she finishes the prayer. If she performs tayammum and does not pray, the right of raj'ah remains by istihsan, and this is their position. According to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, it does not remain, and this is qiyas, because tayammum in the absence of water is treated like ghusl due to the permissibility of prayer, entering the mosque, and other rules. There is no difference between the permissibility of a prayer performed and the permissibility of undertaking it, since each requires purification. So if tayammum is like ghusl in respect of rulings, then likewise in this ruling, indeed more so, because caution is observed in ending raj'ah. Zufar and the three said the same. Their argument (the two shaykhs): tayammum is a necessary purification, being actually a soiling, because it does not remove ritual impurity with certainty; if water is found later, the person was in a state of impurity from the previous event. It is only made a purification by legal fiction due to the need to perform prayer, so that duties are not multiplied upon her, and what is established by necessity is limited to its extent. (Ayni, Fath) And she prays with tayammum an obligatory or voluntary complete prayer in the most correct view. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether tayammum, performed by a woman whose menstrual blood has stopped for less than ten days and who cannot find water, ends the husband's right of raj'ah. The matn states 'or she performs tayammum and prays' – but there is disagreement on the effect. Two positions are attributed to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, or the two shaykhs usually are Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; but here 'ash-shaykhayn' might refer to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad is separate). The passage says: it is said that raj'ah ends by the mere commencement of prayer according to the two shaykhs, but the correct view is that it does not end until she finishes the prayer. If she only performs tayammum without praying, the right of raj'ah remains by istihsan according to them. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan disagrees with the two shaykhs; he holds that raj'ah ends immediately upon tayammum by qiyas, because tayammum is like ghusl in all rulings. The two shaykhs respond that tayammum is a necessary purification, not a perfect one, so its effect is limited. The passage concludes that she must pray a complete prayer (obligatory or voluntary) with tayammum for it to take effect. Zufar and the three (other schools) agree with Muhammad's qiyas.

Key Terms
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that abandons strict qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling due to necessity or custom.
رجعة (raj'ah): The right of a husband to take back his wife during the waiting period (iddah) after a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) without a new marriage contract.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive a ruling for a new case based on a similar case with a known ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), when does raj'ah end for a woman who performs tayammum after her menstruation stops for less than ten days?
A) Immediately after performing tayammum
B) Only after she finishes a complete prayer with that tayammum
C) Only after she performs ghusl later
D) It never ends

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, tayammum does not automatically end raj'ah; it requires completing a full prayer to trigger that effect, per the two shaykhs' istihsan-based preference.

Incomplete Ghusl and Its Effect on Raj'ah
Ruling on incomplete ghusl and its effect on raj'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on missing spots in ghusl to determine whether raj'ah is cut off, distinguishing between a full limb and less than a limb.

Core Idea
Missing less than a limb in ghusl cuts off raj'ah because dryness spreads quickly; missing a full limb does not.

When a woman in her third menstrual 'iddah performs ghusl but forgets a spot, the validity of her ghusl and its effect on raj'ah depends on whether the missed area is a full limb or smaller. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, its basis in istihsan, and the practical consequences for the woman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ghusl after the third menstruation. The ruling on raj'ah (the right to take back the wife) depends on the size of the missed spot.
If she forgets a part of her body, does raj'ah end or continue?
2
Apply the size test
Distinguish between missing 'less than a limb' (e.g., a finger, a small patch) and 'a full limb or more' (e.g., an arm, leg). If less than a limb, raj'ah is cut off; if a limb or more, raj'ah remains.
'Udw is defined as a complete limb like an arm, leg, or head.
3
Understand the istihsan reasoning
The qiyas (analogy) would either cut off raj'ah for a full limb (because she washed most) or keep it for less than a limb (because impurity is indivisible). Istihsan prefers the distinction based on drying speed: small areas dry quickly, so water effectively reached them; large areas do not.
Istihsan is a Hanafi method that opts for a more practical outcome over strict analogy.
4
Apply the precautionary rule
Even if raj'ah is cut off (less than a limb missed), she cannot remarry or be approached until she washes that spot or a prayer time passes. This is a precaution (ihtiyat) regarding private parts.
The ruling on marriage prohibition is separate from the ruling on raj'ah.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on mouth and nose
Rinsing the mouth (madmadah) and sniffing water (istinshaq) are considered less than a limb according to Imam Muhammad, so missing them cuts off raj'ah. Abu Yusuf disagrees.
The text follows Muhammad's view as the sound (sahih) one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اغتسلت : أي المعتدة من الحيضة الثالثة. (مص) تنقطع: [ أي الرجعة؛ لأنه قليل يتسارع إليه الجفاف. (ع)] أي إن اغتسلت ونسيت شيئاً من بدنها لم يصبه الماء إن كان أقل من عضو انقطعت الرجعة؛ لأن تسارع الجفاف إليه بعد أصابته بالماء غير بعيد لاسيما في الحر الشديد لكن لا يحل لها التزوج، وكذا لا يحل قربانها حتى تغسله احتياطاً في أمر الفروج، أو يمضي عليها وقت صلاة، وإن كان عضواً فما فوقها لا تنقطع؛ لأنه كثير لا يتسارع إليه الجفاف وهذا استحساناً، والقياس في العضو الكامل أن تنقطع الرجعة؛ لأنها غسلت الأكثر ، وله حكم الكل، وفيه قياس آخر أن الرجعة تبقى في ما دون العضو أيضاً؛ لأن حكم الحدث لا يتجزأ زوالاً كما لا يتجزأ ،ثبوتاً، فبقيت على ما كانت قبل الاغتسال، وجه الاستحسان، وهو الفرق بين العضو وما دونه أن ما دون العضو يتسارع إليه الجفاف لقلته. (مستخلص، فتح) ولو عضواً لا: أي لا تنقطع، وكل واحد من المضمضة والاستنشاق كالأقل؛ لأنهما عضو واحد على الصحيح عند محمد بله خلافاً لأبي يوسف (ط، ع) ولو طلق أي الرجل امرأته وهي ذات إلخ. (ع) أو ولدت: امرأته قبل الطلاق في عصمة في مدة يتصور أن يكون منه بأن ولدت لستة أشهر فصاعداً . من يوم التزوج. (ع) راجع: أي له أن يراجعها في المسألتين؛ لأن الحبل في المسألة الأولى يعني من طلق امرأته وهي حامل، وقال: لم أجامعها متى ظهر في مدة يتصور يكون منه بأن ولدت لستة أشهر فصاعداً جعل منه؛ لقوله عليه: "الولد للفراش" فكان ذلك دليل الوطء، وكذا في المسألة الثانية أي من طلق امرأته وولدت قبل الطلاق حيث يثبت نسبه منه فيتأكد الملك، والطلاق في الملك المتأكد يعقب الرجعة وبطل زعمه بتكذيب الشرع، ولهذا يثبت به الإحصان. وإنما قيدنا بقولنا: "قبل الطلاق"؛ لأنها لو ولدت بعده تنقضي به العدة، فتستحيل الرجعة. فإن قلت: قوله : "لم أطأها" صريح في عدم الجماع وثبوت النسب دلالة الجماع، والصريح فوق الدلالة فكان أولى، قلت: الدلالة من الشارع أقوى من العبد لاحتمال الكذب عنه دون الشارع؛ وإذا كانت الولادة لأقل من أشهر لا يكون له الرجعة لعدم ثبوت النسب فلم يصر مكذبا شرعا. (عيني، فتح، مستخلص) خلا بها: أي بامرأته خلوة صحيحة وأغلق باباً أو أرخي ستراً. (ط،ع)
She performed ghusl: i.e., the woman observing 'iddah from the third menstruation. (Mas) It is cut off: [i.e., raj'ah], because it is a small area to which dryness quickly spreads. (A) i.e., if she performed ghusl and forgot some part of her body that water did not reach, if it is less than a limb, raj'ah is cut off, because dryness quickly reaches it after water contacts it, especially in intense heat, but she is not permitted to marry, nor is it allowed to approach her until she washes it as a precaution regarding private parts, or until a prayer time passes. If it is a limb or more, it is not cut off, because it is a large area to which dryness does not quickly spread, and this is istihsan. The qiyas for a complete limb is that raj'ah is cut off because she washed the majority, and it has the ruling of the whole. Another qiyas: raj'ah remains even for less than a limb, because the ruling of ritual impurity is indivisible in removal as it is indivisible in establishment, so it remains as it was before ghusl. The basis of istihsan is the distinction between a limb and less than a limb: less than a limb dries quickly due to its smallness. (Mukhtasar, Fath) And if a limb, no: i.e., it is not cut off. Each of rinsing the mouth and sniffing water is like the lesser, because they are one limb according to the sound view of Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. (T, A) And if he divorces (i.e., a man his wife) while she is pregnant, etc. (A) or she gave birth: his wife before the divorce, while in his marriage, within a period in which the child could be from him, i.e., she gave birth after six months or more from the day of marriage. (A) He can take her back: i.e., he has the right to revoke his divorce in both issues, because the pregnancy in the first issue (i.e., he divorced his wife while she was pregnant, and he said 'I did not have intercourse with her') whenever it appears within a period in which it could be from him, i.e., she gives birth after six months or more, it is attributed to him due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The child belongs to the bed,' so that is evidence of intercourse. Similarly in the second issue (i.e., he divorced his wife and she gave birth before the divorce) where his lineage is established from him, so the marital authority is confirmed, and divorce in confirmed authority brings about raj'ah, and his claim is nullified because the law contradicts him. Therefore, confining to prison is established for him. We qualified by 'before the divorce' because if she gives birth after the divorce, the 'iddah ends with the birth, so raj'ah becomes impossible. If it is said: his statement 'I did not have intercourse with her' is explicit in denying intercourse, and the establishment of lineage implies intercourse, and the explicit is above implication, so it should take precedence. The response: the implication from the Lawgiver is stronger than from the slave, because lying is possible from the slave but not from the Lawgiver. And if the birth is at less than six months, he does not have the right of raj'ah because lineage is not established, so he is not contradicted by the Law. (Ayni, Fath, Mukhtasar) He secluded with her: i.e., with his wife in a valid seclusion, closing a door or lowering a curtain. (T, A)
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage combines two distinct rulings. First, regarding ghusl: if a woman performing ghusl from the third menstruation forgets to wash a part of her body, the effect on raj'ah depends on the size. If the missed spot is less than a full limb (e.g., a finger or small area), raj'ah is cut off because that small area would dry quickly after water contacts it, implying she has effectively completed ghusl. However, as a precaution regarding marriage and intercourse, she must still wash the spot or wait for a prayer time to pass. If the missed spot is a full limb (e.g., an arm) or more, raj'ah is not cut off because such a large area does not dry quickly. This ruling is based on istihsan, preferring practical over strict analogy. Two analogical arguments exist: one says raj'ah should be cut off if she washed most of the limb, the other says it should not be cut off because impurity is indivisible. The istihsan chooses to differentiate based on drying speed. Also, rinsing the mouth and sniffing water are considered less than a limb according to Muhammad, so if missed, raj'ah ends, but Abu Yusuf differs. Second, regarding a pregnant woman: if a man divorces his wife while she is pregnant, or she gives birth before the divorce, and he claims he did not have intercourse with her, his claim is rejected because the child is attributed to him by the hadith 'The child belongs to the bed' if born six months or more after marriage. Therefore, he can still perform raj'ah. The legal principle: the sharia's implication (through the hadith) is stronger than the husband's explicit denial. If she gives birth after less than six months, lineage is not established, so he cannot be forced into raj'ah. The passage also mentions that valid seclusion (khalwa) is considered like actual intercourse in some rulings.

Key Terms
عضو ('udw): A limb or major body part; in this context, a full limb like an arm or leg.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that gives priority to a more practical outcome over strict analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Raj'ah is cut off because she did not complete ghusl.
B) Raj'ah is not cut off because the missed area is a full limb.
C) Raj'ah is cut off but she must still wait for a prayer time.
D) Raj'ah remains only if she washes the limb again.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of missing a part in ghusl on raj'ah depends on whether the missed area is a full limb or smaller: less than a limb cuts raj'ah, a limb or more does not, based on istihsan.

Raj'ah for the Pregnant Woman: The Authority of the Sharia
Ruling on raj'ah for pregnant woman when husband denies intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a husband cannot deny raj'ah when his wife gives birth to a child within six months of marriage, applying the hadith 'al-waladu li al-firash'.

Core Idea
The sharia's implication through the birth of a child overrides the husband's explicit denial of intercourse.

If a man divorces his pregnant wife and claims he never had intercourse, can he still take her back (raj'ah)? The Hanafi school rules that he can, because the child's attribution to the marriage bed legally proves intercourse. This lesson examines the proof and the counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A man divorces his wife while she is pregnant (or she gives birth before the divorce). He says he did not have intercourse. The wife had the child within six months or more from the marriage.
The six-month period is the minimum duration of pregnancy according to Hanafi law.
2
Identify the husband's claim
The husband asserts explicitly that he did not have intercourse. This is clear (sarih) and would normally be taken as true.
In legal matters, a person's own statement is usually given weight.
3
Identify the counter-evidence from sharia
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The child belongs to the bed.' This hadith attributes the child to the husband if born within a possible timeframe. This is a stronger authority than the husband's word.
The hadith is mutawatir in meaning, so its transmission is certain (qat'i al-thubut).
4
Resolve the conflict
The sharia's implication (dalalah) is stronger because it is from the Lawgiver, who cannot lie. The husband may be lying. Therefore, the birth establishes intercourse legally, so the child is attributed to him, and his divorce is revocable (he can take her back).
The explicit statement of a person is overridden by the implicit evidence from the sharia.
5
Apply the exception
If the child is born before six months from marriage, lineage is not established, so the husband's denial stands, and he cannot be forced into raj'ah.
This is because the child could not be from him.

Source Text

She performed ghusl: i.e., the woman observing 'iddah from the third menstruation. (Mas) It is cut off: [i.e., raj'ah], because it is a small area to which dryness quickly spreads. Each of rinsing the mouth and sniffing water is like the lesser, because they are one limb according to the sound view of Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. (T, A) And if he divorces (i.e., a man his wife) while she is pregnant, etc. (A) or she gave birth: his wife before the divorce, while in his marriage, within a period in which the child could be from him, i.e., she gave birth after six months or more from the day of marriage. (A) He can take her back: i.e., he has the right to revoke his divorce in both issues, because the pregnancy in the first issue (i.e., he divorced his wife while she was pregnant, and he said 'I did not have intercourse with her') whenever it appears within a period in which it could be from him, i.e., she gives birth after six months or more, it is attributed to him due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The child belongs to the bed,' so that is evidence of intercourse. Similarly in the second issue (i.e., he divorced his wife and she gave birth before the divorce) where his lineage is established from him, so the marital authority is confirmed, and divorce in confirmed authority brings about raj'ah, and his claim is nullified because the law contradicts him. Therefore, confining to prison is established for him. We qualified by 'before the divorce' because if she gives birth after the divorce, the 'iddah ends with the birth, so raj'ah becomes impossible. If it is said: his statement 'I did not have intercourse with her' is explicit in denying intercourse, and the establishment of lineage implies intercourse, and the explicit is above implication, so it should take precedence. The response: the implication from the Lawgiver is stronger than from the slave, because lying is possible from the slave but not from the Lawgiver. And if the birth is at less than six months, he does not have the right of raj'ah because lineage is not established, so he is not contradicted by the Law. (Ayni, Fath, Mukhtasar) He secluded with her: i.e., with his wife in a valid seclusion, closing a door or lowering a curtain. (T, A)
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage combines two distinct rulings. First, regarding ghusl: if a woman performing ghusl from the third menstruation forgets to wash a part of her body, the effect on raj'ah depends on the size. If the missed spot is less than a full limb (e.g., a finger or small area), raj'ah is cut off because that small area would dry quickly after water contacts it, implying she has effectively completed ghusl. However, as a precaution regarding marriage and intercourse, she must still wash the spot or wait for a prayer time to pass. If the missed spot is a full limb (e.g., an arm) or more, raj'ah is not cut off because such a large area does not dry quickly. This ruling is based on istihsan, preferring practical over strict analogy. Two analogical arguments exist: one says raj'ah should be cut off if she washed most of the limb, the other says it should not be cut off because impurity is indivisible. The istihsan chooses to differentiate based on drying speed. Also, rinsing the mouth and sniffing water are considered less than a limb according to Muhammad, so if missed, raj'ah ends, but Abu Yusuf differs. Second, regarding a pregnant woman: if a man divorces his wife while she is pregnant, or she gives birth before the divorce, and he claims he did not have intercourse with her, his claim is rejected because the child is attributed to him by the hadith 'The child belongs to the bed' if born six months or more after marriage. Therefore, he can still perform raj'ah. The legal principle: the sharia's implication (through the hadith) is stronger than the husband's explicit denial. If she gives birth after less than six months, lineage is not established, so he cannot be forced into raj'ah. The passage also mentions that valid seclusion (khalwa) is considered like actual intercourse in some rulings.

Key Terms
الفراش (al-firash): The marital bed; metaphor for the marriage itself.
دلالة (dalalah): Implication or indication, as opposed to explicit statement (sarih).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, if a woman gives birth 8 months after marriage, and the husband claims he never had intercourse, what is the consequence?
A) The husband's claim is accepted because he knows best.
B) Raj'ah is possible because the child is attributed to the bed.
C) The divorce is final and she cannot be taken back.
D) The wife must provide witnesses to prove intercourse.

Key Takeaway
When a child is born within six months of marriage, the sharia's evidence of paternity overrides the husband's denial, allowing raj'ah.

Divorce of Pregnant Wife with Claim of No Intercourse
Ruling on revocability when husband denies intercourse after divorcing a pregnant wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a husband can revoke his divorce of a pregnant wife even if he denies intercourse, provided the child is legally attributed to him, and cite the hadith and the principle of takdhib al-shar'i.

Core Idea
A husband's denial of intercourse is overridden by the shari'a if a child is born within a viable period.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi family law: a husband divorces his pregnant wife and then claims he never had intercourse with her. At first glance, such a denial might seem to preclude any possibility of revocation (raj'ah). However, the Hanafi school, relying on the prophetic hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash' and the principle that the Lawgiver's indication is stronger than a human's statement, rules that the husband can still revoke if the child is legally attributed to him. This lesson unpacks the proof, the conditions, and the exceptions to this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Husband's Claim
The husband divorces his pregnant wife (or one who has already given birth) and explicitly states that he never had intercourse with her. This creates a conflict: his explicit denial versus the fact of pregnancy or childbirth.
The two scenarios are: (1) divorce during pregnancy, (2) divorce after birth but before the divorce.
2
Apply the Proof: The Hadith and Its Legal Weight
The hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash' attributes the child to the husband (the marital bed). This is a strong indication (dalala) that intercourse took place, even if the husband denies it. The Hanafi school treats this indication as outweighing the husband's explicit statement because the Lawgiver cannot lie.
The hadith is mutawatir in meaning, so it is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala in this specific application.
3
Establish the Condition for Attribution
The child's attribution requires that the birth occurs within a viable pregnancy period from the time of marriage. According to Hanafi law, the minimum is six months from the marriage contract. If the birth is six months or more, the child is legally the husband's.
If the birth is less than six months, lineage is not established, so the husband's denial stands.
4
Apply the Principle of Takdhib al-Shar'i
When the shari'a's indication contradicts a person's claim, the shari'a is given precedence. Here, the child's attribution to the husband proves intercourse, so his denial is 'belied' by the Lawgiver. Therefore, he is considered to have had intercourse and retains the right to revoke.
This principle also applies to other areas, such as establishing ihsan (married state for penalties).
5
Note the Exception: Birth After Divorce or Insufficient Period
If the wife gives birth after the divorce (i.e., during the iddah), the iddah ends with the birth, making raj'ah impossible. If the birth is less than six months from marriage, lineage is not established, so the husband's denial is accepted and he cannot revoke.
The text restricts 'before the divorce' to clarify that birth after divorce ends the iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو طلق أي ذات حمل أو ولدت وقال : لم أطأها راجع، أي له أن يراجعها في المسألتين؛ لأن الحبل في المسألة الأولى يعني من طلق امرأته وهي حامل، وقال: لم أجامعها متى ظهر في مدة يتصور يكون منه بأن ولدت لستة أشهر فصاعداً جعل منه؛ لقوله عليه: "الولد للفراش" فكان ذلك دليل الوطء، وكذا في المسألة الثانية أي من طلق امرأته وولدت قبل الطلاق حيث يثبت نسبه منه فيتأكد الملك، والطلاق في الملك المتأكد يعقب الرجعة وبطل زعمه بتكذيب الشرع، ولهذا يثبت به الإحصان. وإنما قيدنا بقولنا: "قبل الطلاق"؛ لأنها لو ولدت بعده تنقضي به العدة، فتستحيل الرجعة. فإن قلت: قوله : "لم أطأها" صريح في عدم الجماع وثبوت النسب دلالة الجماع، والصريح فوق الدلالة فكان أولى، قلت: الدلالة من الشارع أقوى من العبد لاحتمال الكذب عنه دون الشارع ؛ وإذا كانت الولادة لأقل من أشهر لا يكون له الرجعة لعدم ثبوت النسب فلم يصر مكذبا شرعا.
And if he divorces a pregnant woman or one who gave birth and said: 'I did not have intercourse with her', he may revoke. That is, he has the right to revoke her in both cases because the pregnancy in the first case (he divorces his wife while she is pregnant and says: I did not have intercourse with her) whenever it appears within a period possible to be from him, such as she gives birth at six months or more, it is considered from him due to his statement عليه: 'The child belongs to the bed', so that is evidence of intercourse. Similarly in the second case: he divorces his wife and she gave birth before the divorce, where his lineage is established from him, so the marital bond is confirmed, and divorce in a confirmed bond is followed by raj'ah, and his claim is invalidated by the shari'a's contradiction. Therefore, it establishes ihsan. And we restrict by our saying 'before the divorce' because if she gave birth after it, the 'iddah would end with it, making raj'ah impossible.
