I say: The indication from the Lawgiver is stronger than from the slave because lying is possible from him but not from the Lawgiver. And if the birth is for less than months, he does not have raj'ah because lineage is not established, so he is not contradicted by the shari'a.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a husband divorces his pregnant wife or a wife who has already given birth, and he claims that he never had intercourse with her. According to Hanafi law, the husband can still revoke the divorce if the child is attributed to him by the shari'a. The key proof is the hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash' (the child belongs to the bed), meaning the child is legally attributed to the man who is her husband. If she gives birth at six months or more from the time of marriage (the minimum viable pregnancy period), the child is considered his, establishing that intercourse must have occurred. This contradicts his claim, so he is not believed in his own favor. However, the commentary notes that if she gives birth after the divorce, the 'iddah ends, so no raj'ah is possible. If the birth occurs for less than six months, lineage is not established, so his claim stands and he cannot revoke. The passage also addresses a potential objection: the husband's explicit statement should override the inference from birth. The response is that the shari'a's indication is stronger because it does not lie, whereas a human may lie. Thus, the child's attribution takes precedence.

Key Terms
الفراش (al-firash): The marital bed, a metaphor for the husband's authority over the wife; the child is attributed to him.
الإحصان (al-ihsan): The state of being married and having had sexual intercourse, which affects certain penalties (e.g., stoning for zina).
التكذيب الشرعي (al-takdhib al-shar'i): The shari'a contradicts a person's statement based on a stronger proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) No, because pregnancy after divorce ends the 'iddah.
D) Yes, only if she also admits intercourse.

Key Takeaway
A husband's denial of intercourse is not accepted if a child is born within a viable period of marriage; the child's attribution to him by the shari'a grants him the right to revoke the divorce.

Khalwah, Denial of Intercourse, and Revocation: The Lesser Evil Principle
Ruling with rational proof and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on revocation after khalwah and denial of intercourse, and apply the exception when a child is born within two years.

Core Idea
Denial after khalwah blocks revocation, but birth of a child within two years retroactively validates it—choosing the lesser evil.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a nuanced scenario: a husband who validly secludes with his wife, then denies intercourse, then divorces. Normally, he cannot revoke because the marital bond is confirmed only by actual intercourse. But what if he revokes anyway and the wife later gives birth? The Hanafi ruling employs a remarkable legal reasoning: to avoid the greater sin of zina, the shari'a treats the child as proof of pre-divorce intercourse, validating the revocation even though the husband initially denied it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
The text first states that after khalwah and denial of intercourse, if the husband divorces, he cannot revoke because the marital bond is only confirmed by actual intercourse. The admission of no intercourse is binding on himself.
This is based on the principle of iqrar (admission) being binding on the declarant.
2
Understand the reasoning for the initial rule
The dower is confirmed due to khalwah, but that does not prove intercourse. The husband's denial is accepted in his own favour regarding revocation because it is an admission, and the shari'a does not contradict him without evidence.
The rule separates the effects of khalwah: it confirms dower but not intercourse for all purposes.
3
Identify the exception and its condition
Despite the initial rule, if the husband revokes and the wife gives birth within two years of the divorce, the revocation is validated retroactively. The child's lineage from the husband establishes that conception occurred before the divorce.
The two-year period is the maximum duration of pregnancy in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply the legal principle of choosing the lesser evil
The text addresses an objection: why is lying (the husband's earlier denial) not prevented? The answer: lying is a lesser evil than zina. To avoid declaring the child illegitimate or post-divorce intercourse as zina, the shari'a treats the husband as having had intercourse before the divorce, thereby overriding his denial.
This is the principle of 'ahwan al-baliyatayn' (choosing the lesser of two evils).
5
Note the practical outcome
The revocation becomes valid, the child is legitimate, and the husband is deemed to have lied in his earlier statement—but this is preferred over the alternative of zina.
This ruling is specific to Hanafi fiqh; other schools may not permit such retroactive validation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طلقها لا:[ أي لا يراجع وليس له الرجعة؛ لأنها تثبت في الملك المتأكد بالوطء وقد أنكر فيصدق في حق نفسه. ...] أي وإن خلا بامرأته ثم قال: "لم أجامعها" ثم طلقها، فلا رجعة له عليها؛ لأن الملك إنما يتأكد بالوطء وقد أقر بعدمه، فيصدق في حق المهر؛ لأنه إقرار والإقرار حجة قاصرة على نفسه وليس مكذباً شرعاً؛ لأن تأكد المهر يبتني على تسليم البدل، وهو التخلية ورفع الموانع، والعدة تجب احتياطاً لاحتمال الوطء فلم يكن بوجوب العدة، وتأكد المهر قضاء بالدخول. (مستخلص، فتح) راجعها: أي إن طلقها بعد ما خلا بها وقال لم أجامعها ثم راجعها (ط) عامين أي سنتين من وقت الطلاق. (ع) صحت تلك الرجعة لأن العدة لما وجبت ثبت نسب الولد منه، وظهر أن العلوق كان سابقا على الطلاق فنزل واطيا فيكون به مكذبا شرعا . (ع)] أي فإن راجعها مع أنه لم تكن له الرجعة فجاءت بولد لأقل من سنتين حكم بصحة تلك الرجعة؛ لأنه يثبت النسب منه؛ لأنها لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها والولد تبقى في البطن في هذه المدة، فجعل واطياً قبل الطلاق لا بعده؛ لأنه لو لم يطأها قبل الطلاق لزال الملك بنفس الطلاق فيكون الوطء بعد الطلاق حراماً، فيجب صيانة فعل المسلم عنه، فإذا جعل واطيا قبل الطلاق تصح الرجعة بعده، فصار مكذبا شرعاً في قوله: " لم أجامعها". فإن قيل: إن الكذب أيضاً حرام فلم لا يجب صيانة المسلم عنه. لكن الكذب أهون من الزنا، ومن ابتلي ببليتين يختار أهونهما لا محالة.
Then if he divorces her, no: he cannot revoke and has no right of raj'ah because the marital bond is confirmed by intercourse and he has denied it, so he is believed in his own favour... That is, if he secluded with his wife then said: 'I did not have intercourse with her', then divorced her, he has no raj'ah over her because the bond is only confirmed by intercourse and he has admitted its absence, so he is believed in respect of the dower because it is an admission, and an admission is a proof limited to himself, and he is not contradicted by the shari'a because the confirmation of the dower is based on handing over the substitute, which is seclusion and removal of barriers, and the 'iddah is obligatory as a precaution due to the possibility of intercourse, but it was not by obligation of 'iddah that the dower is confirmed as a judicial decision of consummation... He revoked her: meaning he divorced her after seclusion and said 'I did not have intercourse with her', then he revoked her... two years from the time of divorce... That revocation is valid because when the 'iddah was obligatory, the child's lineage from him is established, and it becomes clear that the conception was before the divorce, so he is deemed to have had intercourse and thus is contradicted by the shari'a... So if he revoked her even though he had no right of raj'ah, and she brought a child within less than two years, it is judged that the revocation is valid because the lineage is established from him; since she did not admit that her 'iddah had ended, and the child remains in the womb for this period, he is considered to have had intercourse before the divorce, not after, because if he did not have intercourse before the divorce, the marriage bond would have ended with the divorce, and then intercourse after the divorce would be forbidden, and it is necessary to protect the Muslim's action from that. So when he is considered to have had intercourse before the divorce, the revocation after it is valid, and he becomes contradicted by the shari'a in his statement 'I did not have intercourse with her'. If it is said: Lying is also forbidden, so why is the Muslim not protected from that? We say: Lying is less serious than zina, and one who is afflicted with two afflictions inevitably chooses the lesser.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is in two parts. The first part (from 'ثم طلقها لا' to 'قضاء بالدخول') discusses a man who has khalwah (valid seclusion) with his wife, then says he did not have intercourse with her, and then divorces her. The ruling is that he cannot revoke the divorce because the marital bond is only confirmed by actual intercourse. Since he admits no intercourse, his admission is binding on himself, and the shari'a does not contradict him because seclusion alone does not prove intercourse. The dower is confirmed due to seclusion, but that is a different matter. The second part (from 'راجعها' onwards) addresses a scenario where, despite lacking the right to revoke, he does revoke her, and she later gives birth within two years of the divorce. In that case, the revocation is validated retroactively. The reasoning: the child's lineage is established from him, proving that conception occurred before the divorce. To avoid concluding that zina occurred after divorce, the shari'a treats him as having had intercourse before the divorce, thereby contradicting his earlier denial. The passage ends with a theological point: lying is forbidden, but it is the lesser evil compared to zina, so the shari'a chooses to consider him a liar to avoid the graver sin.

Key Terms
الخلوة (al-khalwah): Valid seclusion between husband and wife with privacy and no impediment.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission; a person's statement against his own interest, binding on himself.
أهون البليتين (ahwan al-baliyatayn): Choosing the lesser of two evils; a legal principle used here to prefer lying over zina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
After khalwah and denial of intercourse, revocation is blocked, but a child born within two years retroactively validates the revocation, applying the principle of choosing the lesser evil.

Revocation by Subsequent Birth After Conditional Divorce
Ruling on revocation by second birth after conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a second birth after a conditional divorce constitutes a revocation (raj'ah) based on the interval between births, applying the Hanafi condition of a minimum six-month pregnancy period.

Core Idea
A second birth is a revocation only if from a new pregnancy, proven by a six-month gap.

In Hanafi family law, a conditional divorce tied to childbirth raises questions about subsequent births. This lesson examines when a second birth revokes the divorce through implicit intercourse during the 'iddah. The key is whether the second child is from a new pregnancy or the same one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
The husband says: 'If you give birth, you are divorced.' This suspends divorce on the condition of childbirth.
2
Analyze the first birth
When the wife gives birth, the condition is fulfilled, divorce takes effect immediately, and her 'iddah begins.
The 'iddah for a divorced pregnant woman ends with delivery, but here we are considering a subsequent birth.
3
Examine the interval between births
Determine if the second birth occurs six months or more after the first. If yes, the second child is from a new conception after the divorce, and the birth implies intercourse during 'iddah, which is a revocation.
Hanafi minimum pregnancy period is six months (based on Quran 46:15).
4
Apply the exception for short intervals
If the interval is less than six months, the second child is from the same pregnancy; no new conception occurred. Thus, the second birth does not revoke the divorce; instead, the 'iddah ends with the second birth.
The 'iddah ends with delivery of all children from the same pregnancy.
5
Consider additional factors
The revocation holds even if the second birth occurs more than two years after the first, as long as the wife does not admit that her 'iddah has ended. Also, the rule applies even if the husband said 'Whenever you give birth...' for multiple births.
The principle of attributing children to the husband supports this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ولداً ثم ولدت من بطن آخر فهي رجعة. كلما ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثلاثة في بطون. أي من قال لامرأته: إن ولدت فأنت طالق، فولدت ولداً طلقت ثم ولدت من بطن أخرى أي بعد ستة أشهر فالولادة الثانية رجعة؛ لأنه وقع عليها الطلاق بالولادة الأولى لوجود الشرط ووجبت العدة عليها، فيكون الولد الثاني من علوق حادث لوجود أقل مدة الحمل فيحمل على أنه منه فيكون مراجعة بالوطء الحادث وإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين ما لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها، بخلاف ما إذا كان بين الولدين أقل من ستة أشهر، فإنه لا يكون مراجعة؛ لأن الثاني ليس بحادث بعد الولد الأول؛ لأن الطلاق وقع عليها بالولد الأول، وهي حامل بالثاني، فتنقضي بوضعه العدة.
And if she gives birth, you are divorced; then she gives birth to a child, then gives birth from another womb: it is a revocation. Whenever she gives birth, you are divorced; then she gives birth to three in wombs. That is, whoever said to his wife: If you give birth, you are divorced, and she gave birth to a child, she is divorced. Then she gave birth from another womb, i.e., after six months, then the second birth is a revocation because the divorce fell upon her with the first birth due to the existence of the condition, and the 'iddah became obligatory on her. So the second child is from a new conception due to the existence of the minimum period of pregnancy, and it is considered to be from him, so it becomes a revocation by the new intercourse, even if she comes with it after more than two years as long as she does not admit the end of her 'iddah. Unlike if there is less than six months between the two births, then it is not a revocation because the second is not new after the first; because the divorce fell upon her with the first child while she was pregnant with the second, so the 'iddah ends with the delivery of the second.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage discusses a conditional divorce: a man says to his wife, 'If you give birth, you are divorced.' She gives birth, so the divorce takes effect, and her 'iddah begins. If she then gives birth again, that second birth is considered a revocation (raj'ah) by way of new intercourse, provided there is at least six months between the two births (the minimum period for a new pregnancy). The reasoning: the first birth triggers the divorce and 'iddah. The second child, born after at least six months, is a new conception that occurred after the divorce. Since the child is attributed to the husband, it implies that he had intercourse with her during the 'iddah, which constitutes a revocation. This holds even if the second birth occurs more than two years after the first, as long as she does not admit that her 'iddah has ended. However, if the interval is less than six months, the second child is considered conceived before the divorce (since it is the same pregnancy), so the second birth does not constitute a revocation; rather, the 'iddah ends with the delivery of the second child. The passage also mentions the sentence 'كلما ولدت فأنت طالق فولدت ثلاثة في بطون' which illustrates a similar case where the condition is repeated for each birth, and the birth of three children in separate pregnancies results in multiple divorces and revocations.

Key Terms
البطن الآخر (al-batn al-akhar): Another pregnancy, i.e., a separate gestation.
المراجعة بالوطء (al-muraja'ah bi al-wat'): Revocation of divorce by engaging in sexual intercourse during the 'iddah.
أقل مدة الحمل (aqall muddat al-haml): The minimum period of pregnancy according to Hanafi school: six months.

D) No, because the first birth already ended the marriage.

Key Takeaway
The second birth after a conditional divorce is a revocation only if it results from a separate pregnancy, proven by a minimum six-month interval between births.

Ruj'ah Validity Despite Denial of Intercourse: The Proof of Childbirth
Ruling and proof for ruj'ah despite husband's denial of intercourse when birth occurs within two years.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ruj'ah when a husband denies intercourse after khalwa but a child is born within two years, and explain the istihsan-based proof.

Core Idea
A child born within two years of divorce legally proves prior intercourse, making ruj'ah valid despite the husband's denial.

In Hanafi law, ruj'ah (revocable divorce revocation) normally requires that the husband's ownership of the wife be confirmed through actual intercourse. If a husband secludes with his wife but claims he never consummated, then divorces her, he is believed regarding his own rights and loses the right to ruj'ah. However, if the wife gives birth within two years of the divorce, the child's lineage is established from him, legally proving that intercourse must have occurred before the divorce. This ruling uses istihsan (juristic preference) to choose the lesser harm (possible lying) over the greater harm (presuming fornication after divorce).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
The husband secludes with his wife (khalwa), then claims he did not have intercourse. He then divorces her. The default ruling is that he has no right to ruj'ah because ownership is not confirmed without actual intercourse, and his denial is accepted regarding his own right.
This is based on the principle that admission is proof limited to oneself.
2
Recognize the exception: birth within two years
If the wife gives birth to a child within less than two years from the date of divorce, the ruj'ah becomes valid. The birth changes the legal assessment.
The two-year period is the maximum duration of pregnancy in Hanafi law.
3
Trace the legal reasoning: lineage proves prior intercourse
The child's lineage is established from the husband because the wife did not acknowledge the completion of her 'iddah, and the child remains in the womb for up to two years. This legally proves that intercourse occurred before the divorce, contradicting the husband's denial.
Once lineage is established, the husband is considered to have had intercourse prior to divorce (faj'ala wati'an qabla al-talaq).
4
Understand the proof: istihsan (juristic preference)
The proof is based on choosing the lesser of two harms. If the husband were treated as not having had intercourse, the child would be illegitimate and the husband would be guilty of fornication if he later had intercourse. To avoid the greater harm (zina), the law presumes that the husband had intercourse before the divorce, even if that means he is regarded as having lied.
The text argues: 'lying is less severe than fornication, and whoever is afflicted with two afflictions inevitably chooses the lesser of them.'

Source Text

(مستخلص) قلنا: نعم
Then if he divorces her once, then if he takes her back and she gives birth after that within less than two years, that ruj'ah is valid. Meaning the woman, meaning after the ruj'ah. Then he divorces her no: meaning he does not take her back and there is no ruj'ah for him; because (the right of ruj'ah) is established in the confirmed ownership by intercourse, and he has denied it, so he is believed regarding his own right. Another explanation: If he has secluded with his wife and then said: 'I did not have intercourse with her' and then divorced her, there is no ruj'ah for him upon her; because ownership is only confirmed by intercourse, and he has acknowledged its absence, so he is believed regarding the dower; because it is an admission and admission is a proof limited to oneself, and he is not legally contradicted; because the confirmation of the dower depends on the delivery of the compensation, which is seclusion and removal of barriers, and the 'iddah is due as a precaution due to the possibility of intercourse, so it was not due to the obligation of 'iddah, and the confirmation of the dower is a judicial ruling of actual entry. So if he takes her back despite not having the right of ruj'ah, and she comes with a child within less than two years, the ruling is that that ruj'ah is valid; because lineage is established from him; because she has not acknowledged the completion of her 'iddah and the child remains in the womb during this period, so he is considered as having had intercourse before the divorce, not after; because if he had not had intercourse before the divorce, ownership would have ceased by the divorce itself, and intercourse after the divorce would be forbidden, and one must protect the Muslim from that, so when he is considered as having had intercourse before the divorce, the ruj'ah after it is valid, so he becomes legally contradicted in his statement: 'I did not have intercourse with her.' If it is said: Lying is also forbidden, so why is one not required to protect the Muslim from it? But lying is less severe than fornication, and whoever is afflicted with two afflictions inevitably chooses the lesser of them. We say: yes.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof, addressing a scenario where a husband secludes with his wife, then claims he did not consummate, and later divorces her. Normally, after such a divorce with no consummation, the husband has no right of ruj'ah because ownership is not confirmed without intercourse. The husband's denial is accepted regarding his own rights (like dower). However, if the wife gives birth within two years of the divorce, the child's lineage is established from the husband, legally proving that intercourse occurred before the divorce. This overrides the husband's denial as a legal contradiction. The law presumes that the husband would not commit the greater harm (fornication after divorce) but would accept the lesser harm (lying), so the birth validates the ruj'ah.

Key Terms
رجعة (ruj'ah): The revocation of a revocable divorce, allowing the husband to resume marital relations without a new contract within the 'iddah period.
خلوة (khalwa): A valid seclusion between spouses where no legal impediment to intercourse exists, even if intercourse did not actually occur.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying, to ensure no pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The husband changing his statement and admitting intercourse.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a child born within two years of a divorce legally proves prior intercourse, validating the ruj'ah even if the husband denied consummation.

Conditional Divorce and Multiple Births: The Separate Belly Rule
Rulings on multiple births after a conditional divorce and their effect on ruj'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between separate bellies and a single belly in a conditional divorce and state whether a subsequent birth constitutes a ruj'ah.

Core Idea
Only births from separate pregnancies (≥6 months apart) constitute ruj'ah; births from the same pregnancy do not.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce triggered by childbirth. The key distinction is whether the child is from a new pregnancy (separate belly) or the same pregnancy. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining ruj'ah and the number of divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conditional Divorce Scenario
Recognize the phrase 'If you give birth, you are divorced' and note that the divorce is conditional upon birth.
The condition must be clear and unambiguous.
2
Determine the Time Gap Between Births
Measure the interval between the first and second birth. If it is six months or more, the births are from separate bellies; if less, they are from the same belly.
Minimum pregnancy duration in Hanafi fiqh is six months.
3
Apply the Six-Month Rule for Ruj'ah
If the gap is ≥6 months, the second birth constitutes a ruj'ah (revocation) and also triggers another divorce due to the condition. If <6 months, no ruj'ah occurs.
The ruj'ah happens automatically through the new intercourse implied by the separate pregnancy.
4
Analyze Effects on 'Iddah and Subsequent Divorces
After the first birth, 'iddah begins. A separate pregnancy resets the 'iddah. Subsequent births in separate bellies continue the pattern of ruj'ah and divorce. After the third divorce, the woman becomes permanently forbidden.
The 'iddah after the final birth is by months (or by birth if pregnant).

When she gives birth to the second, another divorce falls upon her, and her 'iddah remains as it is because she is pregnant with the third. Then when she gives birth to the third, her 'iddah ends by his birth, and nothing falls upon her even if the condition is met, because divorce does not occur coinciding with the end of 'iddah. For this reason, if she did not give birth to the third, the second also would not occur due to the end of 'iddah by the second.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage states the rulings for a conditional divorce where the husband says 'If you give birth, you are divorced.' When the wife gives birth, the condition is met and one divorce occurs. If she then gives birth again after at least six months (separate pregnancy), that second birth constitutes a ruj'ah (revocation) because it is from a new intercourse, so the husband effectively took her back. This second birth also triggers another divorce due to the condition. Similarly, a third birth in a separate pregnancy constitutes another ruj'ah and a third divorce, after which she becomes permanently unlawful. If births occur within less than six months (same pregnancy), they do not constitute ruj'ah because the second child is part of the same pregnancy and the 'iddah ends with the last birth. The passage carefully distinguishes between separate bellies (multiple pregnancies) and one belly (single pregnancy with multiple children).

Key Terms
بطن (batn): A pregnancy or a belly; used to distinguish between multiple pregnancies (separate bellies) and one pregnancy (single belly).
علوق ('uluq): Conception or pregnancy.
رجعة (ruj'ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce during the 'iddah period, restoring the marriage without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The second birth is not a ruj'ah; the third birth is a ruj'ah.

D) Neither the second nor third birth is a ruj'ah.

Key Takeaway
In a conditional divorce triggered by childbirth, only births from separate pregnancies (≥6 months apart) constitute ruj'ah; births within the same pregnancy do not.

Conduct of Revocably Divorced Woman and Husband's Rights
Rulings on revocably divorced woman's conduct and husband's obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a revocably divorced woman's adornment, the husband's entry etiquette, and the travel dispute, and explain the evidence from Quran 65:1.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman remains a wife; adornment is encouraged but travel requires explicit ruj'ah.

This lesson covers three key rulings for the revocably divorced woman and her husband. First, the woman may adorn herself to encourage reconciliation. Second, the husband should announce his entry to avoid accidental ruj'ah through a disliked act. Third, he may not travel with her without revoking the divorce. A notable intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar on travel highlights how the Quranic prohibition is interpreted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sub-rulings
List the rulings on: (1) adornment of the woman, (2) husband's entry without notice, (3) traveling with her. Note the hukm level for each.
Adornment is mustahabb; entry without notice is makruh; travel without ruj'ah is haram/makruh tahrimi.
2
Analyze the conditions for adornment
Adornment is recommended only if reconciliation is hoped for and the husband is present. If she knows he despises her or if he is absent, she should not adorn herself.
The marriage bond is partially intact; adornment is a means to encourage ruj'ah.
3
Examine the husband's entry etiquette
The husband should not enter suddenly; he should announce his presence (e.g., by clearing throat) even if he intends ruj'ah. This prevents ruj'ah occurring through a disliked act (looking at private parts).
Al-Kanz's absolute statement is preferred over al-Durr's restriction.
4
Understand the dispute on travel
The majority Hanafi position prohibits travel without explicit ruj'ah; if he does not explicitly renounce ruj'ah, travel itself may be considered de facto ruj'ah. Zufar permits it because the marriage bond remains.
The majority rely on Quran 65:1: 'Do not turn them out of their houses.'
5
Apply the Quranic evidence
The verse is interpreted as applying to revocable divorce; the prohibition of expulsion implies staying in the marital home. Travel without ruj'ah contradicts this command.
The context of the verse includes the possibility of ruj'ah (لعلى الله يحدث بعد ذلك أمرا).

[رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٨/١] والثالث رجعة ويقع ا الطلاق الثالث بولادة الولد الثالث ووجبت العدة بالأقراء ولا سبيل إلى الرجعة. (ط) تتزين: [ أي في العدة؛ لقيام النكاح إذا كانت المراجعة مرجوة وكان الزوج حاضراً، ويحرم ذلك في البائن في عدة الوفاة. (ط)] أي تجلو وجهها وتصقل خديها في العدة؛ لأنها حلال للزوج؛ إذ النكاح قائم بينهما، والرجعة مستحبة، والتزيين باعث عليها، فيكون مشروعاً. وهذا إذا كانت المراجعة مرجوة، أما إذا كانت تعلم أنه يراجعها لشدة بغضها، فإنها لا تفعل ذلك، وكذا إذا غاب الزوج. (مستخلص، مسكين) حتى يؤذنها : [ أي يعلمها بدخوله بخفق النعل والتنحنح ونحو ذلك. (ع)] قيده في "الدر" بما إذا لم يقصد رجعتها تبعا للهداية، وإطلاق المصنف أولى؛ لأنه قد تقع المراجعة بالنظر إلى داخل فرجها وهو مكروه فيندب أن لا يدخل عليها حتى يؤذنها ولو قصد الرجعة؛ دفعا لوقوع الرجعة بالمكروه، وصرح الولوالجي بالإطلاق. (فتح) حتى يراجعها: وهذا إذا صرح بعدم رجعتها، فلو لم يصرح كان السفر رجعة دلالة، وقال زفر: له أن يسافر بها؛ لأن النكاح بينهما قائم فصار كأنه لم يطلقها، ولأن المسافرة تكون رجعة دلالة لكونها حراماً بدون المراجعة للنهي عن الإخراج والخروج، فظاهر حاله اجتناب المحرم فصار كالوطء في النكاح الموقوف، ولنا قوله تعالى: ولا تُخْرِجُوهُنَّ مِنْ بُيُوتِهِنَّ الله (الطلاق: ۱)، نزلت في الطلاق الرجعي بدليل سياقه، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَطَلَقُوهُنَّ (الطلاق: ۱)؛ لأن الطلاق معقب للرجعة وهو المراد بقوله تعالى: لَعَلَّ الله يُحْدِثُ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ أَمْرا (الطلاق: ۱) أي يحدث المراجعة بأن تبدو له المراجعة بعد صريح الطلاق. (فتح)
So the second and third children are a ruj'ah, and the revocably divorced woman adorns herself, and it is recommended that he not enter upon her until he informs her, and that he not travel with her until he takes her back. The revocably divorced woman, i.e., the husband of the revocably divorced woman, i.e., it is recommended for the husband. So the second and third children: meaning by them the previous ruj'ah becomes apparent... [continuation of previous explanation] ...and the third is a ruj'ah, and the third divorce falls by the birth of the third child, and the 'iddah becomes by the months, and there is no way to ruj'ah. She adorns herself: meaning during the 'iddah; because the marriage is established if reconciliation is hoped for and the husband is present, and it is forbidden in the case of irrevocable divorce and the 'iddah of death. Meaning she unveils her face and polishes her cheeks during the 'iddah; because she is lawful for the husband; since the marriage remains between them, and ruj'ah is recommended, and adornment encourages it, so it is permissible. This is when reconciliation is hoped for; if she knows that he will not take her back due to intense hatred, she does not do that, and likewise if the husband is absent. Until he informs her: meaning he informs her of his entry by the sound of sandals, clearing the throat, and the like. It is restricted in al-Durr to when he does not intend his ruj'ah following al-Hidayah, but the absolute statement of the author (al-Kanz) is better; because ruj'ah may occur by looking at her private part, which is disliked, so it is recommended that he not enter upon her until he informs her, even if he intends ruj'ah, to avoid ruj'ah occurring through a disliked action. Al-Walwālijī declared it absolute. Until he takes her back: This is when he explicitly states that he will not take her back; if he does not state, then travel is a de facto ruj'ah. Zufar said: He may travel with her; because the marriage is established between them, so it is as if he did not divorce her, and because traveling with her is a de facto ruj'ah since it is forbidden without ruj'ah due to the prohibition of taking her out and her leaving, so his apparent situation is that he avoids the forbidden, so it is like intercourse in a suspended marriage. Our proof is the Almighty's saying: 'Do not turn them out of their houses' (al-Talaq 65:1), which was revealed concerning revocable divorce as indicated by its context, which is His saying: 'When you divorce women' (al-Talaq 65:1); because divorce is followed by ruj'ah, and that is the meaning of His saying: 'Perhaps Allah will afterward bring about an event' (al-Talaq 65:1), i.e., He brings about reconciliation, meaning that reconciliation appears to him after explicit divorce.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage addresses the conduct of both spouses during revocable divorce. The woman may adorn herself to encourage ruj'ah, but only if reconciliation is hopeful; if hopeless or husband absent, it is not done. The husband is advised not to enter upon her suddenly; he should announce his presence to avoid an accidental ruj'ah through looking at her private parts. Traveling with her is not allowed unless he explicitly or implicitly revokes the divorce. There is a dispute with Zufar, who permits travel, but the Hanafi view prohibits it based on the Quranic verse (65:1) which implies the woman should remain in the marital home. The passage also includes the conclusion of the previous topic regarding the third birth as a ruj'ah.

Key Terms
مطلقة رجعية (mutallqah raj'iyyah): A woman divorced with a revocable divorce (first or second) who is still within her 'iddah and can be taken back without a new marriage contract.
إخراج (ikhraj): Expelling or taking the wife out of the marital home during 'iddah, which is prohibited for revocably divorced women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the husband advised to announce his entry upon his revocably divorced wife?
A) To avoid startling her.
B) To prevent an accidental ruj'ah by looking at her private parts.
C) Because she is no longer his wife.
D) To ensure she is properly covered.

Key Takeaway
A revocably divorced woman may adorn herself to encourage ruj'ah, but the husband must avoid actions that lead to sinful travel or accidental ruj'ah.

Multi-Birth Scenarios in Revocable Divorce
Ruling with reasoning on multi-birth divorce scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on multiple births in revocable divorce and apply the distinction between separate bellies versus one belly.

Core Idea
Each birth from a new pregnancy indicates a revocable return and triggers a new divorce, up to the third.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a complex scenario: a woman is revocably divorced while pregnant and then gives birth to multiple children from separate pregnancies. The ruling hinges on whether the births are from the same pregnancy (one batn) or different pregnancies (multiple butun). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the law of revocable divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial condition
The woman is revocably divorced while pregnant. The first birth triggers the first divorce because the condition (birth) is met. Note that the divorce was suspended on the birth, so it occurs automatically at the first delivery.
2
Trace the effect of a second birth after six months
If she gives birth to a second child from a separate pregnancy (i.e., after at least six months), this proves that the husband had revoked the divorce (raj'ah) after the first birth. Thus a second divorce occurs at the second birth.
3
Apply the same logic to a third birth
A third child from yet another pregnancy indicates a second revocation, leading to a third and final irrevocable divorce. After this, no further revocable return is possible.
4
Distinguish multiple births from the same pregnancy
If twins or triplets are born from one pregnancy (less than six months apart), only the first child triggers a divorce; the subsequent children are part of the same 'iddah period and do not cause additional divorces. The 'iddah ends with the last delivery.
This distinction is captured by the phrase 'في بطون' in the source text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالولد الثاني والثالث رجعة والمطلقة الرجعية تتزين، وندب أن لا يدخل في الطلاق الأول أي في الطلاق الثاني عليها حتى يؤذنها، ولا يسافر بها حتى يراجعها، المطلقة الرجعية أي الزوج المطلقة الرجعية أي استحب للزوج فالولد الثاني إلخ: أي يظهر بهما الرجعة السابقة؛ لأن بولادة الأول وقع الطلاق لوجود الشرط فصارت عدتها بالإقرار، ثم إذا جاءت بولد آخر من بطن آخر بأن جاءت به بعد ستة أشهر ولو لأكثر من سنتين ما لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها، علم أنه من علوق حادث فتثبت به الرجعة، وتقع طلقة أخرى رى بولادته بوجود الشرط وتكون عدتها بالأقراء، ثم إذا جاءت بالثالث تبين أنه كان راجعها بعد وقوع الثانية وتقع طلقة ثالثة بولادة الثالث فتحرم عليه حرمة مغلظة، وتكون عدتها بالأقراء، ولو جاءت بعد ذلك بولد في بطن آخر لا تثبت به المراجعة؛ لعدم تصورها حقيقة أو حكماً، ولا يثبت نسبه منه؛ لأن وطأها حرام عليه. وقوله: " في "بطون احتراز عما إذا كانوا في بطن واحد وهو ما إذا كان بين الولادتين أقل من ستة أشهر؛ لأنها بوضع الأول تقع عليها طلقة؛ لوجود الشرط وهي حامل بالثاني والثالث فتكون عدتها بوضع الحمل، وإذا وضعت الثاني تقع عليها طلقة أخرى، وعدتها باقية على حالها؛ لأنها حامل بالثالث، ثم إذا وضعت الثالث انقضت عدتها بولادته ولم يقع عليها شيء وإن وجد الشرط؛ لأن الطلاق لا يقع مقارنا لانقضاء العدة، ولهذا لو لم تلد الثالث لم تقع الثانية أيضاً لانقضاء العدة بالثاني. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٨/١] والثالث رجعة ويقع ا الطلاق الثالث بولادة الولد الثالث ووجبت العدة بالأقراء ولا سبيل إلى الرجعة.
So the second and third child are [signs of] revocable return. And the revocably divorced woman adorns herself, and it is recommended that [the husband] does not enter upon her in the first divorce, i.e. in the second divorce, until he announces to her, and he should not travel with her until he revokes her. The revocably divorced woman i.e. the husband of the revocably divorced woman i.e. it is recommended for the husband. So the second child etc.: i.e., the two [children] manifest the earlier revocable return; because by the birth of the first, divorce occurred due to existence of the condition, so her 'iddah became by menstrual periods, then if she comes with another child from another belly, i.e. she gives birth after six months even if more than two years as long as she did not acknowledge completion of her 'iddah, it is known that it is from a new conception, so revocable return is established by it, and another divorce occurs [؟] by his birth by existence of the condition, and her 'iddah becomes by menstrual periods, then when she comes with the third, it becomes clear that he had revoked her after the occurrence of the second, and a third divorce occurs by the birth of the third, so she becomes prohibited with severe prohibition, and her 'iddah becomes by menstrual periods. And if after that she comes with a child in another belly, revocable return is not established by it due to lack of conceiving it actually or legally, and his lineage is not established from him because intercourse with her is forbidden to him. And his saying: "in bellies" is a caution against when they are in one belly, i.e. when less than six months are between the two births; because by the birth of the first, one divorce occurs upon her due to existence of the condition while she is pregnant with the second and third, so her 'iddah becomes by delivering the pregnancy. When she delivers the second, another divorce occurs upon her, and her 'iddah remains as it was because she is pregnant with the third. Then when she delivers the third, her 'iddah ends with his birth and nothing occurs upon her even if the condition exists because divorce does not occur coinciding with the end of 'iddah. Hence, if she did not give birth to the third, the second would not occur either due to the end of 'iddah with the second. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/268] And the third is a revocable return and the third divorce occurs by the birth of the third child, and 'iddah becomes obligatory by menstrual periods, and there is no way to revocable return.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning (talil) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses a scenario where a woman is revocably divorced [during pregnancy] and subsequently gives birth to multiple children from separate pregnancies. The key principle is that the arrival of each child from a new pregnancy (after at least six months between births) indicates that a revocable return (raj'ah) occurred before that birth, thereby triggering a new divorce. The first divorce occurs at the first birth (due to the condition attached), and the 'iddah becomes by menstrual periods (since the divorce occurred post-birth). If a second child comes from a separate pregnancy, this proves the husband had revoked the first divorce, so a second divorce occurs at the second birth. Similarly, a third child indicates a second revocable return, leading to a third divorce, which is irrevocable. The passage distinguishes between multiple children from separate bellies (batn) versus one belly (e.g., twins). For twins born less than six months apart, only the first birth counts as a divorce; the second and third children are still part of the same pregnancy, so no additional divorces occur, and 'iddah ends with the final delivery. The Hanafi school bases this on the principle that divorce occurs only when the condition is met while the marriage is intact; once 'iddah ends, no further divorce can occur.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): Revocable return, where the husband takes back his wife during the 'iddah without a new marriage contract.
الأقراء (al-aqra'): The menstrual periods that constitute the 'iddah of a revocably divorced woman who is not pregnant.
البطن (al-batn): A pregnancy or the period of gestation culminating in a birth; a 'belly' refers to a separate pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman is revocably divorced while pregnant. She gives birth to a child (first birth). Then, after 8 months, she gives birth to another child (second birth). According to this passage, what is the legal effect of the second birth?
A) The second birth is ignored for divorce purposes.
B) The second birth proves a revocable return occurred, so a second divorce takes effect.
C) The second birth makes the marriage continue without divorce.
D) The second birth is considered part of the first pregnancy, so no second divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
In revocable divorce, each new birth from a separate pregnancy proves a revocable return and triggers a further divorce, up to the third; births from the same pregnancy do not cause multiple divorces.

Conduct with Revocably Divorced Wife: Adornment, Entry, Travel
Rulings on adornment, entering, and travel with revocably divorced wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on adornment, entry announcement, and travel for the revocably divorced wife, explain the proof from Quran 65:1, and evaluate the dispute with Zufar.

Core Idea
Revocable 'iddah: marriage persists – she adorns, he announces entry, travel requires revocation.

This lesson examines three practical rules for the revocably divorced wife and her husband. The key legal principle is that the marriage bond continues during the 'iddah, so certain behaviors are recommended or prohibited to preserve the possibility of reconciliation. The rulings are derived from the Quranic prohibition of expulsion (65:1) and the concept of 'dalalah' (indication) used by the Hanafis to determine revocable return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts and their rulings
List the three actions discussed: adornment (tazayyun), announcement of entry (idhn), and travel (safar). For each, determine whether it is sunnah, makruh, or haram according to the Hanafi school.
Note that the rulings vary based on intention and context.
2
Locate the proof for each ruling
Identify the Quranic verse (65:1) cited for the prohibition of travel without revocation. Understand how the verse is applied to the other rulings through analogy and general principles.
The verse prohibits expulsion, which forms the basis for the travel ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah for travel
Show how the prohibition of expulsion implies that taking the wife out of the home (even by travel) is prohibited unless revocable return (raj'ah) has occurred. This is the key evidential link.
4
Understand the ikhtilaf with Zufar
State Zufar's position that travel is permissible because the marriage subsists. Then explain the Hanafi response: travel would be an expulsion and is only allowed if it constitutes a dalalah of revocable return.
5
Apply the concept of dalalah
Recognize that in Hanafi fiqh, implicit actions (like travel without explicit denial) can serve as legal indications (dalalah) of revocable return, thereby changing the ruling.
This is a distinctive Hanafi feature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ط)] أي تجلو وجهها وتصقل خديها في العدة؛ لأنها حلال للزوج؛ إذ النكاح قائم بينهما، والرجعة مستحبة، والتزيين باعث عليها، فيكون مشروعاً. وهذا إذا كانت المراجعة مرجوة، أما إذا كانت تعلم أنه يراجعها لشدة بغضها، فإنها لا تفعل ذلك، وكذا إذا غاب الزوج. (مستخلص، مسكين) حتى يؤذنها : [ أي يعلمها بدخوله بخفق النعل والتنحنح ونحو ذلك. (فتح)
She adorns herself: [i.e. during the 'iddah; because the marriage subsists if the return is hoped for and the husband is present, and it is prohibited for the bain [divorced woman] in the 'iddah of death. (T)] i.e. she brightens her face and smoothes her cheeks during the 'iddah; because she is lawful for the husband since the marriage subsists between them, and revocable return is recommended, and adornment encourages it, so it is prescribed. This is when return is hoped for; however, if she knows that he will not return her because of intense hatred, she should not do that, and likewise if the husband is absent. (Mustakhlas, Miskin) Until he announces to her: [i.e. he informs her of his entry by the sound of sandals, clearing the throat, and the like. (Ayni)] He restricted it in al-Durr to when he does not intend her return, following al-Hidayah, but the absolute wording of the author (al-Sahib) is more appropriate; because revocable return may occur by looking at the inside of her vagina, which is disliked, so it is recommended that he not enter until he announces to her even if he intends return; to avoid the occurrence of return through a disliked act, and al-Walwaliji declared the absolute [view]. (Fath) Until he revokes her: This is when he explicitly states that he will not return her; if he does not explicitly state, then travel would be a dalalah (indication) of return. Zufar said: He may travel with her because the marriage subsists between them, so it is as if he had not divorced her; and because traveling with her would be a dalalah of return since it is prohibited without return due to the prohibition of expulsion and going out, so his apparent state is avoidance of the forbidden, so it becomes like intercourse in suspended marriage. And our proof is the saying of Allah: "And do not expel them from their houses" (65:1) revealed regarding revocable divorce as indicated by its context, i.e. His saying: "When you divorce women" (65:1); because divorce is followed by return, and that is meant by His saying: "Perhaps Allah will bring about after that a [new] matter" (65:1), i.e. He brings about return, that return appears to him after explicit divorce. (Fath)
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran, covering three aspects of the revocably divorced woman's 'iddah: adornment, husband's entry, and travel. First, the revocably divorced woman (mutallaqah raj'iyyah) is encouraged to adorn herself during the 'iddah because the marriage bond persists and revocable return (raj'ah) is recommended. This is conditioned on hope of return; if return is not expected, adornment is not recommended. Second, the husband is recommended not to enter upon her without announcing his entry, even if he intends to revoke, to avoid a revocable return occurring through a disliked act (e.g., looking at her private parts). Some scholars restrict this to when he does not intend return, but the preferred view is absolute announcement. Third, the husband should not travel with her until he explicitly revokes the divorce. If he does not explicitly state intention, travel itself becomes an indication of revocable return. The Hanafi proof is the Quranic verse prohibiting expulsion of women from their homes during revocable divorce, indicating the marriage is ongoing. Zufar's opposing view is that travel is permitted because the wife is still his wife, but the Hanafis argue that travel would be a prohibited expulsion unless return is effected.

Key Terms
المراجعة (al-muraja'ah): Revocable return; the husband taking back his wife during the 'iddah without a new contract.
الدلالة (al-dalalah): An indirect indication that establishes a legal effect (e.g., travel implying revocable return).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband revocably divorces his wife. He wishes to travel with her but does not explicitly state intention to revoke. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He may travel with her as she is still his wife.
B) He must first explicitly revoke the divorce; otherwise travel is prohibited and itself constitutes a revocable return.
C) He may travel but it does not affect the divorce.
D) He must gain her consent before traveling.

Key Takeaway
The revocably divorced wife is still considered married; thus she may adorn herself, the husband should announce entry, and travel without revocation is prohibited and itself constitutes revocable return.

Intercourse During Revocable Divorce
Ruling that revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on intercourse during revocable divorce, state the opposing views, and evaluate the basis of the disagreement.

Core Idea
The marriage bond persists during revocable divorce; thus intercourse is halal.

A revocable divorce does not sever the marriage bond; it only initiates a waiting period. The Hanafi school, based on the Quran and scholarly consensus, holds that intercourse during this period is permissible. This lesson examines the evidence and contrasts with the Shafi'i and Maliki views.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi ruling is that intercourse is permissible and no additional dower is required. Identify the source text's claim: 'wal-talaq al-raj'i la yuharrim al-wat'. Note the authority: it is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik hold that intercourse is prohibited because the divorce severs the marriage bond, and ownership of marriage is a condition for intercourse. Their evidence: the divorce is a definitive severing agent.
3
Analyse the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi proof is Quran 2:228: 'And their husbands have more right to take them back'. The term 'husbands' implies ongoing marriage; the right to revoke without consent shows marital authority. Also, there is consensus that the effect of divorce is postponed until the end of the 'iddah.
4
Rebuttal of opponent
If the marriage were severed, the revocable return would be a new marriage requiring the wife's consent, a new dower, and a guardian. Since revocable return requires none of these, the marriage bond continues.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafi: intercourse is permissible (mubah) and does not break the iddah; Shafi'i/Maliki: intercourse is haram and may require expiation. This affects real-life fiqh decisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطلاق الرجعي لا يحرم الوطء. عندنا حتى لو وطئها لا يجب المهر. (ط، ع)] وقال الشافعي: يحرم؛ لأن ملك النكاح شرط للوطء وقد زال لوجود القاطع وهو الطلاق، وبه قال مالك، ولنا أن الزوجية قائمة حتى يملك الزوج مراجعتها من غير رضاها؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَبُعُولَتُهُنَّ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَّ﴾ (البقرة:۲۲۸) أي برجعتهن، والبعولة هم الأزواج. والتسمية حقيقة تستلزم قيام الزوجية، وقيامها يوجب حل الوطء بالإجماع [رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٩/١] ولأن القاطع قد أخر عمله إلى مدة الانقضاء إجماعاً، فإن عنده يثبت الرجعة بالقول دون رضاها كما . آية قيام النكاح؛ إذ لو زال لكانت الرجعة إثباتاً للملك ابتداء فكيف يملك ابتداء النكاح على الأجنبية من غير رضاها، ومن غير مهر ومن غير ولي. (مستخلص)
And revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse. According to us, even if he has intercourse with her, no dower is obligatory. (T, Ayni) And al-Shafi'i said: It is prohibited because ownership of marriage is a condition for intercourse and it has ceased due to the existence of the severing agent, which is divorce; and Malik said the same. And our proof is that the marriage bond subsists to the extent that the husband has the right to revoke her without her consent; due to His saying: "And their husbands have more right to take them back" (2:228) i.e. to return them, and 'husbands' are the spouses. The naming as a reality entails the subsistence of the marriage bond, and its subsistence necessitates the permissibility of intercourse by consensus. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/269] And because the severing agent has postponed its effect until the end of the period by consensus. According to him (the opponent), revocable return is established by statement without her consent, which is a sign of the subsistence of marriage; for if it had ceased, revocable return would be an establishment of ownership anew—how could he establish ownership of a marriage with a foreign woman without her consent, without a dower, and without a guardian? (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i (and Malik) regarding the permissibility of sexual intercourse during a revocable divorce. The Hanafi position is that intercourse is not prohibited and no additional dower is due. The Shafi'i and Maliki position is that it is prohibited because they consider the marriage bond severed by the divorce. The Hanafi proof is the Quranic verse in al-Baqarah (2:228) which states that husbands have more right to take back their wives during revocable divorce, indicating that the marriage bond continues. The naming of the man as a 'husband' is a reality that implies the continuance of marriage. Furthermore, the effect of divorce is postponed until the end of the 'iddah by consensus; if the marriage had been severed, the revocable return would be a new marriage, which would require the wife's consent, a dower, and a guardian, none of which are required for revocable return. This proves the marriage bond is intact.

Key Terms
الطلاق الرجعي (al-talaq al-raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband may return to his wife during the 'iddah without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on sexual intercourse during a revocable divorce?
A) It is prohibited and requires a new dower.
B) It is permissible and no new dower is required.
C) It is permissible only if the husband intends to revoke.
D) It is prohibited unless the wife consents.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that revocable divorce does not sever the marriage bond; therefore, intercourse remains permissible and no additional dower is due.

Conditions for Remarrying a Bainly Divorced Woman
Rulings on when a bainly or thrice-divorced woman becomes permissible to her first husband.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two categories of bain divorce and apply the Hanafi ruling that actual intercourse by a second husband is required for a thrice-divorced woman to become permissible to her first husband.

Core Idea
A thrice-divorced woman is forbidden until a second husband consummates the marriage with actual intercourse.

This passage addresses a critical issue in family law: when a woman divorced irrevocably (bain) can remarry her first husband. The ruling distinguishes between bain divorce of less than three (revocable by new marriage) and three divorces (requiring an intermediary husband). The Hanafi school insists that genuine intercourse, not mere contract, is the gateway to permissibility, and supports this with Quranic proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of bain divorce
Distinguish between a woman divorced bainan with less than three divorces (for a free woman) and a woman divorced three times. The first category allows immediate remarriage by the first husband; the second requires a second husband's intercourse.
For a slave woman, the threshold is two divorces.
2
Locate the condition for the thrice-divorced woman
The text states that the thrice-divorced woman does not become permissible until 'another man has intercourse with her'. Recognize that this is a condition (shart) for her permissibility.
The second husband must be capable of intercourse; a boy approaching puberty (murahiq) suffices if capable.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof from Quran 2:228
The Hanafi school cites the verse 'And their husbands have more right to take them back' to argue that the marriage bond persists during the 'iddah. This supports the permissibility of intercourse with a bainly divorced woman (less than three) during 'iddah.
The proof is for the first scenario, but the principle of continuation of marriage bond underlies the entire section.
4
Note the dispute regarding intercourse in prohibited states
The Hanafi position holds that intercourse during menstruation, postpartum bleeding, fasting, or ihram still fulfills the condition. Malik disagrees, considering such intercourse sinful and ineffective.
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi.
5
Derive the practical ruling for the thrice-divorced woman
Combine the conditions: valid marriage to a second husband, actual intercourse (insertion), and the second husband's capability. The woman becomes permissible to the first husband only after these are met.
If the second husband is impotent or cannot insert, the condition is not fulfilled.

Section on what makes the divorced woman permissible. And [the man] may marry his bain (irrevocably divorced) woman during the 'iddah and after it, not the bain by three [divorces] if free, and by two if slave, until another man has intercourse with her, even if he is [a boy] approaching puberty. i.e. the man i.e. he has intercourse with her i.e. if the woman is bain. According to us, this does not prohibit intercourse: according to us, even if he has intercourse, no dower is obligatory. And al-Shafi'i said: It is prohibited because ownership of marriage is a condition for intercourse and it has ceased due to the existence of the severing agent, which is divorce; and Malik said the same. And our proof is that the marriage bond subsists to the extent that the husband has the right to revoke her without her consent; due to His saying: "And their husbands have more right to take them back" (2:228) i.e. to return them, and 'husbands' are the spouses. (Mustakhlas) Section: Since he mentioned what remedies revocable divorce, he now mentions what remedies non-revocable divorce. (Fath) 'His bain woman': i.e. the woman whom he made bain with less than three [divorces] if free, and with one if slave. (Ayni) 'during the 'iddah and after it': i.e. after its expiry due to the remaining original permissibility, i.e. the number of three [divorces] has not been completed for free woman and two for a slave woman. (Ayni) i.e. if the divorce is bain but less than three, he may marry her during the 'iddah and after its expiry; because the permissibility of the place remains; because its removal is suspended by the third divorce, so it is absent before it. The prohibition for others to marry her during the 'iddah is to avoid confusion of lineage, but there is no confusion regarding the husband because lineage [is from him]. (Mustakhlas) 'Not the bain woman': i.e. he does not marry the woman made bain by three divorces. (Ayni) 'From him': 'Other': i.e. a husband other than the first husband in the definite place. (Ayni) 'Even if approaching puberty': i.e. even if the other is a boy close to puberty, and it is said: whose organ moves and he desires intercourse. (Ayni) And the insane is treated like the sane, likewise the eunuch who is capable of intercourse. And in al-Ghayah: If she marries a beloved and becomes pregnant from him, she becomes permissible to the first, and ihsan is established, contrary to Zufar. And from Abu Hafs: If he does not ejaculate, his lineage is established. Likewise, if she is [a woman whose vagina is] mutilated and he has intercourse, she does not become permissible to the first until she becomes pregnant from the second. And if he has intercourse during menstruation, postpartum bleeding, fasting, or ihram from both or from one of them, she becomes permissible to the first, contrary to Malik. And an old man who cannot have intercourse, if he inserts with the help of his hand, does not make her permissible. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/269] And the one approaching puberty is he who is close to puberty and his organ moves and he desires. It is restricted by the one approaching puberty because on her part the condition of pleasure from both sides, it is said it is estimated at ten years. (Fath)

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with detailed conditions from the section on what makes a divorced woman permissible to her first husband. It covers two scenarios: (1) a woman divorced bainan (irrevocably) but fewer than three times (for a free woman) or twice (for a slave woman) — her husband may remarry her during or after her 'iddah, as the marriage bond is not permanently severed. (2) A woman divorced irrevocably with three divorces (or two for a slave) is forbidden to her first husband until she marries another man and he has actual intercourse with her, even if he is a boy approaching puberty (murahiq) capable of intercourse. The commentary explains that intercourse must be real; if it is invalid (e.g., during menstruation or fasting) some jurists differ, but the Hanafi view is that it still makes her permissible. The other man must be capable of intercourse; an impotent old man inserting with his hand is insufficient. The passage also discusses that a revocably divorced woman is not subject to these restrictions; the earlier discussion on revocable divorce is recapped briefly but the section is mainly about bain divorce.

Key Terms
البائن (al-ba'in): An irrevocably divorced woman; the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
المبانة بالثلاث (al-mubannah bi al-thalath): A woman divorced three times, making her forbidden to the husband until she marries another.
المراهق (al-murahiq): A boy approaching puberty, close to it.

Intercourse During Revocable Divorce 'Iddah: The Hanafi Position
Dispute on whether intercourse is allowed during revocable divorce iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits intercourse during a revocable divorce 'iddah and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki counter-argument using the Quran 2:228 and the principle of deferred effect.

Core Idea
A revocable divorce does not sever the marriage bond; thus intercourse remains permissible during the 'iddah.

A common misconception is that any divorce instantly ends the marital relationship. However, in Hanafi fiqh, a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) leaves the marriage intact until the 'iddah expires. This lesson examines the legal reasoning behind this position and the disagreement with the Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding intercourse during the 'iddah of a revocable divorce. Note the additional point that no dower is due if intercourse occurs.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik forbid intercourse during this period, and understand their key argument: ownership of marriage is a condition for intercourse, and divorce severs that ownership.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Trace the Shafi'i/Maliki logic: the divorce is a 'qati'' (severer) that eliminates the ownership (milk al-nikah), making intercourse unlawful.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school rebuts this using Quran 2:228, which calls the husband 'ba'l' (spouse) and grants him the right to take back his wife without her consent, proving the marriage bond persists. Also note the consensus that the divorce's effect is deferred until the end of the 'iddah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: Hanafis allow intercourse; Shafi'is and Malikis forbid it. This impacts marital relations during the 'iddah period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطلاق الرجعي لا يحرم الوطء. عندنا حتى لو وطئها لا يجب المهر. (ط، ع)] وقال الشافعي: يحرم؛ لأن ملك النكاح شرط للوطء وقد زال لوجود القاطع وهو الطلاق، وبه قال مالك، ولنا أن الزوجية قائمة حتى يملك الزوج مراجعتها من غير رضاها؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَبُعُولَتُهُنَّ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَّ ﴾ (البقرة:۲۲۸) أي برجعتهن، والبعولة هم الأزواج. والتسمية حقيقة تستلزم قيام الزوجية، وقيامها يوجب حل الوطء بالإجماع رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٩/١] ولأن القاطع قد أخر عمله إلى مدة الانقضاء إجماعاً، فإن عنده يثبت الرجعة بالقول دون رضاها كما . آية قيام النكاح؛ إذ لو زال لكانت الرجعة إثباتاً للملك ابتداء فكيف يملك ابتداء النكاح على الأجنبية من غير رضاها، ومن غير مهر ومن غير ولي. (مستخلص)
And revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse. According to us, even if he has intercourse with her, no dower is obligatory. Al-Shafi'i said: It is prohibited because ownership of marriage is a condition for intercourse and it has ceased due to the existence of the severing cause which is divorce, and Malik agreed with him. Our evidence is that the marital relationship remains intact such that the husband has the right to take her back without her consent, due to Allah's saying: 'And their husbands have more right to take them back' (2:228), meaning to take them back in marriage, and 'husbands' are the spouses. The designation as a reality necessitates the existence of the marriage bond, and its existence necessitates the permissibility of intercourse by consensus. And because the severing cause has deferred its effect until the end of the waiting period by consensus, for at that time the right of taking back is established by word without her consent, just as the verse indicates the permanence of marriage; for if it had ceased, taking back would be establishing ownership anew, so how could one initiate marriage with a stranger without her consent, without a dower, and without a guardian? (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the permissibility of intercourse during the waiting period of a revocable divorce. The Hanafi school holds that revocable divorce does not render intercourse unlawful, and even if the husband has intercourse, no additional dower is due. Al-Shafi'i and Malik argue that intercourse is forbidden because the marriage bond is severed by the divorce, making ownership a prerequisite. The Hanafi response relies on Quran 2:228, which calls the husband a 'ba'l' (spouse) and grants him the right to take back his wife without her consent, proving that the marriage bond persists. Additionally, the effect of the divorce is deferred until the end of the waiting period by consensus, so during the 'iddah the marriage is still intact. The Hanafis also argue that if the marriage had ceased, taking back would be a new contract requiring consent, dower, and guardian, which is not the case.

Key Terms
طلاق رجعي (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new contract.
زوجية (zawjiyyah): The marital relationship/bond that persists during revocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is permissible, and no additional dower is due.

D) It is forbidden because the marriage is dissolved.

Key Takeaway
Revocable divorce does not end the marriage; the bond remains, so intercourse is permissible and no additional dower is due.

Conditions for a Thrice-Divorced Woman to Become Lawful
Ruling on the essential conditions for hilla with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four essential conditions for a thrice-divorced woman to become lawful to her first husband and explain the Hanafi proof from Quran and hadith for the requirement of intercourse.

Core Idea
Marriage, intercourse, and 'iddah with a second husband are necessary; mere contract or desire is insufficient.

This lesson unpacks the classical Hanafi position on the hilla process. The text presents the conditions with proofs from the Quran, Sunnah, and ijma', and also addresses exceptions and minority opinions. Understanding these conditions is essential to avoid common misunderstandings about the hilla.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conditions
Read the passage and list the four conditions that must be met for a thrice-divorced woman to become lawful: (1) marriage to a second husband, (2) the marriage must be valid, (3) consummation (intercourse), and (4) completion of the 'iddah from that marriage.
The text explicitly states 'la tuhallu illa bi...'
2
Analyze the proof from Quran (2:230)
The verse 'hatta tankiha zawjan ghayrah' is cited. The Hanafi interpretation is that 'tankiha' implies both marriage contract and consummation, to avoid redundancy. Explain how this proves the requirement of intercourse.
The key linguistic point: the word 'husband' already implies marriage, so 'marry' must mean something additional—consummation.
3
Analyze the proof from Sunnah
The hadith of Rifa'a's wife is presented. The Prophet's statement 'until he tastes your honey and you taste his honey' establishes intercourse as a condition. Discuss the strength of this hadith (sahih) and how it supplements the Quran.
The phrase 'usaylah' (honey) is interpreted as the pleasure of intercourse.
4
Understand the ijma' and exceptions
The passage states that the ummah is unanimous that entry is a condition, with only a few dissenters (Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, Khawarij, etc.) whose views are considered invalid. Note that the condition is insertion, not ejaculation, and that intercourse in prohibited states still fulfills the condition.
The Hanafi position distinguishes between valid khilaf (acceptable disagreement) and invalid opposition (khilaf la ikhtilaf).
5
Recognize invalid alternatives
The text clarifies that intercourse via milk al-yamin (right hand possession) or an invalid marriage (nikah fasid) does not make the woman lawful. Similarly, an old man incapable of intercourse without assistance does not suffice.
The second husband must be capable of intercourse in a normal sense.
