Exception in Ila: 'A Year Except a Day'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ila' with an exception clause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on an oath of ila' containing an exception and explain the distinction between oaths and leases that underlies the ruling.

Core Idea
An oath with an exception is valid despite uncertainty; the excepted day is at the husband's choice.

In the previous lesson, we saw that ila' requires a continuous four-month period of abstention. But what if a husband swears, 'I will not approach you for a year except a day'? Is he immediately a mooli, or does the exception prevent that? The Hanafi school addresses this with a nuanced analysis of how exceptions operate in oaths versus contracts. This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi disagreement between the majority and Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key oath and the legal issue
The husband swears 'I will not approach you for a year except a day.' The question is whether this constitutes an immediate ila' (i.e., does he become a mooli at the moment of the oath?).
2
State the Hanafi position and Zufar's objection
The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) say he is not a mooli immediately. Zufar argues he is a mooli, analogizing to a lease where 'a year except a day' fixes the exception at the end.
Note that Zufar is a Hanafi scholar himself, making this an intra-madhhab dispute.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: why oaths differ from leases
Leases require certainty of term to avoid gharar (prohibited uncertainty). Therefore the exception is forced to the end to validate the contract. Oaths are valid even with uncertainty; thus there is no need to fix the exception. The husband can intend any day as the excepted day.
The key distinction: the presence or absence of a legal requirement for certainty.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the majority view, the husband is not immediately a mooli. He can avoid ila' by simply intending any day of the year as the excepted day. Under Zufar's view, the exception is fixed at the end, so the effective period is 364 days, making him a mooli immediately.
5
Explore additional scenarios in the text
The passage also considers: (a) if he approaches her with four months or more remaining, the exception falls and ila' occurs; (b) if he says 'except a day' without specifying a year, he is not a mooli until he approaches; (c) if he says 'except a day I approach you in it,' he is never a mooli.
These clarify the boundaries of the exception principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا أقربك سنة إلا يوماً أي لم يكن مولياً أيضاً في الحال عندنا، وقال زفر له: يكون مولياً، ويصرف الاستثناء إلى آخر السنة كما لو قال: أجرتك داري سنة إلا يوماً قلنا إنما صرف الاستثناء إلى آخر السنة في الإجارة تصحيحاً للعقد؛ لأنها لا تصح مع الجهالة، بخلاف اليمين فإنها تصح مع الجهالة فلا ضرورة في اليمين إلى صرف الاستثناء إلى آخر السنة، فله أن يجعل اليوم المستثنى أي يوم شاء؛ لأنه منكر فلا يمر به يوم من أيام السنة إلا ويمكنه أن يجعله هو المستثنى، ثم إذا قربها إن بقى من السنة أربعة أشهر أو أكثر صار مولياً لسقوط الاستثناء، وإلا فلا، أي إن بقي أقل منها لم يكن مولياً، ولو حذف سنة وقال: "والله لا أقربك إلا يوماً" لا يكون مولياً حتى يقربها، فإن قربها يصير مولياً، ولو قال: "والله لا أقربك سنة إلا يوماً أقربك فيه" لم يكن مولياً أبداً؛ لأنه استثنى كل يوم يقربها فيه، فلم يتصور منعه أبداً. (عيني، فتح)
"I will not approach you for a year except a day" meaning he is also not a mooli in the immediate situation according to us. Zufar said: he is a mooli, and the exception is referred to the end of the year, just as if he said: I rented you my house for a year except a day. We said: The exception is only referred to the end of the year in leasing to validate the contract; because leasing is not valid with uncertainty, unlike the oath, which is valid with uncertainty, so there is no need in the oath to refer the exception to the end of the year. He may make the excepted day any day he wishes; because it is indefinite, so no day passes of the year except that he can make it the excepted one. Then, if he approaches her while there remain four months or more of the year, he becomes a mooli due to the cancellation of the exception; otherwise not, meaning if less than four months remain, he is not a mooli. If he omitted 'a year' and said: 'By Allah, I will not approach you except for a day,' he is not a mooli until he approaches her; then if he approaches her, he becomes a mooli. If he said: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day in which I approach you,' he is never a mooli; because he excepted every day he approaches her, so his abstinence is never conceivable. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a husband swears: 'I will not approach you for a year except a day.' The Hanafi position (indicated by 'عندنا') is that he is not immediately a mooli. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagrees, arguing that he is a mooli and the exception should be referred to the end of the year, analogous to a lease contract where 'a year except a day' means the term ends one day before the year. The Hanafi response distinguishes between leases and oaths: leases require certainty of the term, so the exception must be fixed at the end; oaths are valid even with uncertainty (jahalah), so the husband may choose any day as the excepted one. Since the excepted day is indefinite (nakirah), he can pick any day, meaning he can always avoid ila' by intending that day. However, if he actually approaches her and at that time four months or more of the year remain, the exception falls away and he becomes a mooli (because he has abstained for four months straight? Actually, the reasoning: if he approaches when less than four months remain, he was not abstaining for a continuous four-month period, so no ila'. If four months or more remain, the exception is effectively canceled, and he had a continuous four-month period from the oath to the approach, thus ila' occurs). The passage also covers: if he says simply 'I will not approach you except a day,' he is not a mooli until he approaches; then he becomes a mooli (meaning he has now sworn to abstain? Actually, that phrasing is odd; the commentator explains that if he omits 'year' and says 'except a day,' it means he has sworn to abstain except for one day, so he is not a mooli until he approaches on that day, after which the oath is broken and he becomes a mooli? This needs careful reading: 'لا يكون مولياً حتى يقربها، فإن قربها يصير مولياً' likely means he is not a mooli until he approaches, because the exception makes the period indefinite; once he approaches, the exception is used, and the remaining period of abstinence (if any) is the new oath? Actually, the Hanafi view: ila' requires a continuous four-month period of abstinence. If he says 'I will not approach you except for a day,' he has not specified a duration, so he is not a mooli. When he approaches on that day, he breaks the oath, but the oath itself was not for a fixed period, so he does not become a mooli? The text says 'يصير مولياً' which is confusing. Possibly it means that after he approaches, the exception is consumed, and the remaining oath becomes an indefinite abstinence, which could become ila' if he then abstains for four months? The commentary is from Ayni and Fath, so it must be understood in that context. Finally, if he says: '...except a day in which I approach you,' he is never a mooli because every day of approach is excepted, so he never actually breaks the oath.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): An exception clause in an oath that excludes a specific day or period from the abstention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband swears: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He is immediately a mooli because the period is effectively 364 days.
B) He is not a mooli immediately; he may choose any day as the excepted day.
C) He is not a mooli because the exception invalidates the oath.
D) He is a mooli only if he does not approach her for exactly four months.

Key Takeaway
An oath with an exception does not create immediate ila' because oaths are valid despite uncertainty; the husband may choose any day as the excepted day, and only if he approaches with four months or more remaining does the exception cancel and ila' occur.

Ila' by Conditional Penalties and from a Revocable Divorcee
Ruling on ila' when husband swears by acts of worship or by divorce, and ila' from a revocably divorced wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for ila' to be valid via conditional penalties, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding specific slave emancipation, and identify the ruling on ila' from a revocable divorcee versus an irrevocably divorced or foreign woman.

Core Idea
Ila' is established by any commitment that effectively deters intercourse, not only by oath in Allah's name.

This passage continues the discussion of ila' (oath of abstention) in the Hanafi school. It addresses two key issues: whether ila' can be formed by conditional penalties such as hajj, fasting, charity, emancipation, or divorce rather than a direct oath, and whether ila' applies to a revocably divorced wife. The text explains the rationale and presents intra-Hanafi differences as well as the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ila'
The passage distinguishes ila' by direct oath and by conditional penalty. Understand that conditional penalties function like a vow (yamīn) because they prevent intercourse.
Conditional penalties are the second type of ila'.
2
List the valid conditional penalties
Valid penalties: hajj, umrah, unspecified fasting, charity, emancipation, divorce. Note that prayer and specified fasting of less than four months are not valid according to the two shaykhs because they are easy to perform.
Specified fasting equal to or exceeding four months is valid.
3
Examine the dispute on specified slave emancipation
Abu Yusuf argues that it is not ila' because the husband can sell the slave and then approach without penalty. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that it is ila' because sale is uncertain.
If the slave is sold, ila' drops by consensus.
4
Analyze the ruling on ila' from a revocable divorcee
A revocable divorcee during 'iddah is still a wife, so ila' applies. The objection that she lacks the right to intercourse is rebutted by the textual basis (nass) maintaining the marital bond.
This is based on the Quranic verse 'from their wives'.
5
Distinguish bain and ajnabiyyah
Ila' from an irrevocably divorced woman (bain) or a foreign woman is invalid because the marriage bond does not exist at the time of the oath. If broken, only expiation applies, not divorce.
The oath is valid for expiation but not for ila'.
6
Evaluate the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that ila' is only by oath in Allah's name. The Hanafi response is that any preventive commitment suffices because the essence is prohibition of intercourse.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا، وإن حلف بحج أو صوم أو صدقة أو عتق أو طلاق أو آلى من المطلقة الرجعية فهو مول، ومن المبانة والأجنبية لا .
لا: أي لا يكون موليا في الصور الثلاث. (ع) وإن حلف شروع في الثاني من نوعي الإيلاء، وهو الحلف بذكر الشرط والجزاء بأن قال: إن قربتك فلله علي حج أو عمرة أو صوم غير معين كيوم وشهر، أما المعين فإن كان بقدر مدة الإيلاء وأكثر كقوله: "فلله علي صوم أربعة أشهر، أولها هذا الشهر فكذلك، أما لو قال: "صوم هذا الشهر" لا يكون مولياً؛ لأنه يمكنه ترك القربان إلى أن يمضي ثم يطأها بلا شيء، ولهذا يكون مولياً بقوله: "فلله علي صدقة أو عتق" سواء كان العتق معيناً كعبدي هذا أو لا، أو كان معلقاً كقوله: "كل مملوك أشتريه فهو . (فتح)
علي
أن
أو صوم: غير معين بأن قال: إن قربتك فلله علي صوم شهر. (ع) أو صدقة: بأن قال: إن قربتك فلله أتصدق بمائة درهم مثلا. (ع) أو عتق بأن قال: إن قربتك فلله علي عتق رقبة. (ع) أو طلاق: بأن قال: إن قربتك فامرأتي طالق هي أو غيرها. (ع) الرجعية بأن قال لها : لا أقربك أربعة أشهر وهي في العدة. (ط) فهو مول: أي في جميع الصور، أما في الشرط والجزاء فلأن هذه الأجزية مانعة من الوطء لما فيها فكانت في معنى اليمين، وأما المطلقة رجعياً فلأنها زوجة فيتناولها النص، فإن قيل: وقوع الطلاق بالإيلاء بطريق المجازاة لكونه ظلمها بمنعها حقها في الجماع، والمطلقة الرجعية ليس لها حق فيه، فكيف يتحقق جزاء الظلم؟ قلنا: إن الحكم في المنصوص مضاف إلى النص لا إلى المعنى والمطلقة الرجعية من نسائنا بالنص لبقاء الزوجية بينهما، وفي عتق العبد المعين خلاف أبي يوسف هو يقول: إن قربانها بلا شيء يلزمه ممكن بأن يبيعه، ثم يقربها وهما يقولان: إن البيع موهوم فلا يمنع الماهية؛ لأن البيع لا يتم به وحده، فربما لا يجد في المدة من يشتريه. ولو باعه سقط الإيلاء بالإجماع؛ لأنه يقدر على قربانها من غير شيء يلزمه، لو قال: إذا قربتك فعلي صلاة لا يكون مولياً عند الشيخين، والصوم المعين كالصلاة عندهما؛ لأنه يسهل إيجادهما فلا يصلحان مانعين، وعند محمد: يكون موليا؛ لأنه قربة، وهو قول أبي يوسف أولا، وقال الشافعي له في أحد قوليه: "لا يكون الإيلاء إلا بالله تعالى" أي لا يكون بالشرط والجزاء. (عيني، فتح) ومن المبانة والأجنبية لا : أي لو آلى من المطلقة البائنة أو آلى من الأجنبية، ثم تزوّجها لم يصر مولياً؛ لأن محل الإيلاء من تكون من نسائنا بالنص؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿لِلَّذِينَ يُؤْلُونَ مِنْ نِسَائِهِمْ﴾ (البقرة:٢٢٦) وهما ليستا منها؛ لأن النكاح في المبانة غير قائم، ولأنه لو صح لوقع به البائن بمضي مدته، والبائن لا يلحق مثله، "ومن الأجنبية" الكلام في مخرجه وقع باطلاً لانعدام المحلية، فلا ينقلب بعد ذلك صحيحاً، فلم يكن مولياً في حق الطلاق دون الكفارة؛ لأن شرط الطلاق باليمين قيام ملك النكاح حال التعليق أو حال وجود الشرط ولم يوجد، فلم يصح بالطلاق، وصح في حق الكفارة. (فتح، مستخلص)
No. And if he swears by Hajj or fasting or charity or emancipation or divorce, or makes ila' from a revocably divorced woman, he is a mooli. From an irrevocably divorced woman and a foreign woman, no.
No: meaning he is not a mooli in the three scenarios. (Ayni) 'And if he swears' is the commencement of the second type of ila', which is the oath mentioning the condition and penalty, such as saying: if I approach you, then upon me is Hajj or Umrah or unspecified fasting like a day or month. As for specified, if it is equal to the ila' period or more, like his saying: 'upon me is fasting for four months, the first of which is this month,' then similarly. But if he said: 'fasting this month,' he is not a mooli; because he can leave off approach until it passes, then have intercourse with no obligation. For this reason, he is a mooli by his saying: 'upon me is charity or emancipation,' whether the emancipation is specified like 'this slave of mine' or not, or is suspended like saying: 'every slave I buy is free.' (Fath) 'upon me' 'that' 'or fasting': unspecified, such as saying: if I approach you, upon me is fasting for a month. (Ayni) 'or charity': such as saying: if I approach you, I must give in charity one hundred dirhams, etc. (Ayni) 'or emancipation': such as saying: if I approach you, upon me is freeing a slave. (Ayni) 'or divorce': such as saying: if I approach you, my wife is divorced, whether her or another. (Ayni) 'the revocable' by saying to her: I will not approach you for four months while she is in the 'iddah. (Talqih) 'he is a mooli': i.e., in all these cases. As for the conditional penalty, because these penalties prevent intercourse due to what they entail, so they are in the meaning of a vow. As for the revocably divorced, because she is a wife, so the text (Qur'an) includes her. If it is said: the occurrence of divorce by ila' is by way of recompense because he wronged her by preventing her right to intercourse, and the revocably divorced woman does not have that right, so how does the recompense occur? We say: the ruling in the text is attached to the text, not to the meaning, and the revocably divorced woman is among 'our women' by the text due to the remaining marital bond between them. Regarding the emancipation of a specified slave, there is a difference of opinion: Abu Yusuf says: approaching her without any obligation is possible by selling the slave, then approaching her. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say: sale is uncertain, so it does not prevent the essence; because sale is not accomplished by itself alone, perhaps he may not find a buyer during the period. If he sells it, ila' drops by consensus; because he can approach her without any obligation. If he says: if I approach you, upon me is prayer, he is not a mooli according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Specified fasting is like prayer according to them; because they are easy to perform, so they are not suitable as preventers. According to Muhammad, he is a mooli; because it is an act of worship, and it was the earlier opinion of Abu Yusuf. Al-Shafi'i said in one of his two opinions: ila' is only by Allah, i.e., it is not by condition and penalty. (Ayni, Fath) 'And from the bain and ajnabiyyah, no': i.e., if he makes ila' from an irrevocably divorced woman or from a foreign woman, then marries her, he does not become a mooli; because the locus of ila' is one who is among 'our women' by the text of the Qur'an: 'For those who swear abstinence from their wives' (2:226). They are not among them; because marriage in the bain is not existing, and because if it were valid, the bain would occur by the passage of the period, and bain does not follow its like. 'And from the foreign woman,' the speech regarding its outcome occurs invalid due to lack of locus, so it does not become valid afterwards. So he was not a mooli with respect to divorce but without expiation; because the condition for divorce by oath is the existence of the marriage bond at the time of suspension or at the occurrence of the condition, and it did not exist, so it was not valid for divorce, but valid for expiation. (Fath, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This lengthy passage from the chapter of ila' covers two major topics: (1) ila' by conditional oaths involving religious acts (hajj, fasting, charity, emancipation) or divorce, and (2) ila' from a revocable divorcee versus an irrevocably divorced or foreign woman. The opening statement ('No. And if he swears...') is a continuation from the previous page, negating something and then affirming that ila' is established in various forms. The commentator (Ayni) explains that 'No' refers to three scenarios where he is not a mooli (likely from the previous discussion). The second type of ila' is the conditional oath: the husband says 'If I approach you, then upon me is Hajj/Umrah/fasting/charity/emancipation/divorce.' These penalties are considered preventive because they impose a heavy obligation, thus they function like a vow of abstention. However, if the penalty is an easy act like prayer or a specified fast of less than four months, it does not constitute ila' because the husband can easily perform the act after approaching. A key dispute is mentioned regarding emancipation of a specific slave: Abu Yusuf argues that the husband can sell the slave and then approach, so it does not prevent intercourse; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say sale is uncertain, so it remains a valid deterrent. The passage also addresses ila' from a revocable divorcee: she is still considered a wife during her 'iddah, so ila' applies. Some might object that the purpose of ila' is to deprive the wife of her right to intercourse, but a revocable divorcee does not have that right. The Hanafi response is that the ruling is based on the text (Qur'an) which includes her due to remaining marital bond, not on the rationale. Finally, ila' from an irrevocably divorced woman (bain) or a foreign woman (ajnabiyyah) is not valid; if he makes such an oath and then marries her, it does not become ila' because the locus (mahall) was absent. However, the oath is still valid in terms of expiation if broken, but not for triggering divorce by ila'.

Key Terms
مطلقة رجعية (muṭallaqa raj'iyya): A wife divorced with a revocable divorce (first or second talaq) who is still in her 'iddah period; the marriage bond continues.
مطلقة بائن (muṭallaqa bā'in): A wife divorced with an irrevocable divorce (third talaq or khul'); the marriage bond is severed.
جزاء (jazā'): The penalty (e.g., hajj, fasting) that becomes obligatory upon breaking the conditional oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'If I approach you, upon me is Hajj.' What is the ruling regarding ila' according to the Hanafi school?
A) He is a mooli because Hajj is a heavy penalty that prevents intercourse.
B) He is not a mooli because it is not an oath by Allah.
C) He is a mooli only if he specifies the year of Hajj.
D) He is not a mooli because Hajj is not a vow.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes ila' in forms beyond the direct oath, including conditional penalties and from revocable divorcees, based on the effective prevention of intercourse and the continued marital bond via 'iddah.

Duration of Ila' for a Slave Woman: Two Months vs. Four Months
Ruling on the duration of ila' for a slave woman with Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of ila' for a slave woman and explain the reason for the difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Ila' duration for a slave woman is two months by analogy to 'iddah.

This lesson addresses a specific ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the duration of ila' (oath of abstention) when the wife is a slave. The ruling depends on whether the period is meant to demonstrate harm or to effect separation. Understanding this difference reveals how legal reasoning (ta'lil) shapes rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the duration of ila' for a slave woman is two months, regardless of whether the husband is free or slave.
This ruling applies generally: the slave woman's status, not the husband's, determines the duration.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that it is four months, the same as for a free woman.
The Shafi'i view treats ila' duration as equal for all wives.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i argues that the period is set to show the husband's harm (zulm) in depriving intercourse, which is equal for free and slave women.
The harm is the same regardless of the wife's status.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that the period is a fixed term for separation (bainuna), analogous to 'iddah. Since 'iddah is halved for slaves, so is ila'.
The key is the purpose: separation vs. harm demonstration.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical effect: a Hanafi husband has two months before automatic divorce; a Shafi'i husband has four months.
The difference matters for determining when divorce occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإيلاء
ومدة إيلاء الأمة شهران، أي الأمة المنكوحة إطلاقه يعمّ ما لو كان زوجها حراً، فلو أعتقت في أثنائها انتقلت إلى مدة الحرائر، وهذا ظاهر في أن الظهار لا يبطل بعتقها بل هو باق وقال الشافعي حله مدة إيلاء الأمة أربعة أشهر، وهذا مبني على أن هذه المدة عنده ضربت لإظهار الظلم بمنع الحق في الجماع والحرة والأمة في ذلك سواء، وعندنا المدة أجلا ضربت للبينونة، فشابهت مدة العدة فتتنصف بالرق لكونها من حقوق النكاح. [تبيين الحقائق: ۱۷۹/۳]
Chapter of Ila'. And the duration of ila' of a slave woman is two months, meaning the married slave woman; its application is general even if her husband is free. If she is emancipated during it, the duration transfers to that of free women. This is apparent in that ila' is not invalidated by her emancipation, but remains. Al-Shafi'i held the duration of ila' of a slave woman to be four months. This is based on his view that this period is set to demonstrate injustice by preventing the right to intercourse, and the free woman and slave woman are equal in that. According to us, the period is set for separation (bainuna), so it resembles the 'iddah period, and thus is halved due to slavery because it is from the rights of marriage.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on the duration of ila' for a slave woman, which is two months. The commentary explains that the rule applies generically even if the husband is free, and if the slave woman is emancipated during the period, it changes to the free woman's duration (four months). The text then presents the Shafi'i school's view that the duration is four months for both free and slave women. The difference stems from the purpose of the period: according to Shafi'i, it is to manifest the husband's harm in depriving the wife of intercourse, which is the same for all wives; thus no reduction for slavery. According to Hanafis, the period is a fixed term for separation, analogous to 'iddah, which is halved for slaves because marriage rights are halved. Hence, Hanafis adopt two months. The passage ends with a citation from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-ila'): An oath by a husband to abstain from intercourse with his wife for a specified period, leading to divorce if no return before expiry.
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave who is married.
المدة (al-muddah): The prescribed period for ila' in this ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Four months
B) Two months
C) One month
D) Six months

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets the ila' duration for a slave woman at two months, halved by analogy to 'iddah, while the Shafi'i school sets it at four months based on the purpose of manifesting harm.

The Two Types of Fay' in Ila': Original and Substitute
Definition and types of fay' in ila'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between original fay' (intercourse) and substitute fay' (verbal declaration) and state the effect of each on the ila' ruling.

Core Idea
Fay' is of two types: original by intercourse and substitute by tongue when unable.

This lesson introduces the concept of fay' (return) in ila'. The husband's oath to abstain from intercourse can be nullified by 'returning' to marital relations. The Hanafi school recognizes two forms: the original act of intercourse and a verbal declaration when intercourse is impossible. Understanding this distinction is essential to grasp the legal effects on the ila' contract.

Guided Path
1
Define fay' linguistically and legally
Start with the linguistic meaning: 'return' (like the shadow returning). Then see how the Quran (2:226) uses it to mean returning from the intention of abstaining. Legally, fay' is the husband's act that nullifies the ila'.
This is the foundation to understand the two types.
2
Identify the two types of fay'
The text says: 'Then fay' is of two types: original and substitute.' The original is intercourse. The substitute is saying with the tongue 'I have returned to her.' Note that the substitute is only valid when the husband is genuinely unable to perform intercourse.
This is the core distinction.
3
Understand the effect of verbal fay'
Verbal fay' nullifies the ila' only regarding the divorce consequence (so that automatic divorce does not occur at expiry of the period). However, it does not nullify the oath itself; if the husband later has intercourse, he must pay expiation (kaffarah) for breaking the oath.
This is different from fay' by intercourse, which nullifies both the divorce and the oath.
4
Contrast with full fay' by intercourse
Full fay' by intercourse nullifies the ila' completely: no divorce at expiry and no expiation for the oath (since the oath is fulfilled, not broken). This is the original and complete form.
This highlights the limited scope of the substitute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففيؤه أن يقول إلخ [ أي رجوع الزوج في هذه الصور. (ط)] اعلم أن الفيء عبارة عن الرجوع يقال: "فاء الظل" إذا رجع، ولما قصد المولى باليمين منع حقها من الوطء سمي الرجوع عنه فيئا، قال تعالى: فَإِنْ فَاءُ والله (البقرة: ٢٢٦) أي رجعوا عن قصدهم، فالفيء في الإيلاء بمنزلة الرجعة في الطلاق. ثم الفيء على نوعين أصل وخلف، والأصل هو الجماع والخلف أن يقول بلسانه فئت إليها، وإنما يصح الفيء باللسان إذا عجز عن الأصل بسبب مرضها أو مرضه ونحوهما والمراد العجز حقيقة، فإن عجز حكماً لا حكم الإيلاء بأن كان محرما لا يكون فيؤه إلا بالوطء؛ لأنه لما كان قادراً حقيقة على الوطء لا يتحول الحكم إلى ما هو خلف عن الوطء. ثم إن قال ذلك أو ما دل عليه كراجعتك وارتجعتك سقط الإيلاء عندنا حتى لا يقع الطلاق بمضي المدة، لكن إذا وطئها بعد المدة أو قبلها بعد زوال الأعذار المذكورة فعليه الكفارة لتحقق الحنث به؛ لأن يمينه باقية في حق إن بطلت في حق الطلاق فالفيء باللسان يبطل الإيلاء في حق الطلاق فقط، والفيء بالوطء يبطله في حق الطلاق والحنث جميعاً، وقال الشافعي له: لا فيء إلا بالجماع، ولا يصح الفيء باللسان أصلا؛ لأنه ظلمها بمنع حقها وهو الوطء، فيكون إيفاؤه به؛ ولأنه لو كان فيئا لكان حنثا؛ لأن المعلق بالفيء حکمان وجوب الكفارة، وامتناع حكم الفرقة، والفيء باللسان لا يعتبر في حق أحد الحكمين، فكذا في حق حكم الآخر، وإليه ذهب الطحاوي، وعن الشافعي حه يقول: ندمت على ما فعلت. أحمد يقول: متى قدرت جامعتك. والأصح ما ما قلنا من أن الفيء باللسان عند العجز خلف عن الوطء لقول علي الله وابن مسعود ه: "يفيء المريض باللسان"، وكفى بهما قدوة؛ ولأن وقوع الطلاق عند مضي المدة باعتبار التعنت والإضرار بها، وذلك ينعدم بالفيء باللسان عند العجز عن الفيء بالجماع؛ لأن الفيء عبارة عن الرجوع، وذلك لا يوجد بهما إلا أن الفيء باللسان مشروط بشرطين عندنا أحدهما أن يكون قوله: فئت أو ما يدل عليه في مدة الإيلاء، والثاني: استمرار العجز من وقت الإيلاء إلى مضي مدة، واشترط أيضاً قيام النكاح وقت الفيء باللسان حتى لو آلى منها وهو قادر، ثم عجز الوطء بعد ذلك لمرض أو بعد مسافة ،وغيرهما، أو كان عاجزاً حين آلى وزال العجز في المدة لم يصح فيؤه باللسان؛ لأنه خلف عن الجماع، فيشترط فيه العجز المستوعب للمدة خلافاً لزفر. (عيني، فتح) فئت إليها : أو أبطلت الإيلاء أو رجعت عنه. (ط) في المدة أي مدة الإيلاء بأن صح أو صحت. (ط) ففيؤه بالوطء أي في الفرج، فلو وطئ في غيره كدبر لا يكون فيئا، وإنما كان فيؤه بالوطء؛ لأنه قدر على الأصل قبل حصول المقصود بالخلف فصار كالمتيمم إذا قدر على الماء قبل أن يصلي، فيبطل التيمم فكذا الفيء باللسان يبطل؛ لأنه خلف عن الجماع. (مستخلص فتح
So his fay' is to say, etc. [i.e., the husband's return in these forms]. Know that fay' means return; it is said 'faa al-zill' when it returns. Since the husband intended by the oath to prevent her right to intercourse, the return from that is called fay'. Allah said 'fain fa'u' (2:226), meaning they returned from their intention. So fay' in ila' is like raj'a in divorce. Then fay' is of two types: original and substitute. The original is intercourse, and the substitute is to say with his tongue 'I have returned to her.' Verbal fay' is valid only if he is unable for the original due to her illness or his illness etc., and the intended is real inability. If there is a legal inability such as being in ihram, his only fay' is intercourse; because he is truly capable. Then if he says that or what indicates it like 'I take you back' or 'I have taken you back', the ila' is nullified according to us so that divorce does not occur upon expiry. However, if he has intercourse after the period or before it after the excuses disappear, he owes expiation due to the breaking of the oath; because his oath remains in that regard, though it is nullified regarding divorce. So verbal fay' nullifies ila' regarding divorce only, while intercourse nullifies it both regarding divorce and the breaking. Al-Shafi'i said: there is no fay' except by intercourse, and verbal fay' is not valid at all; because he harmed her by preventing her right of intercourse, so fulfillment is through it; and because if it were fay', it would be a breach (hanth) requiring expiation, since the rulings attached to fay' are two: obligation of expiation and prevention of separation, and verbal fay' is not considered for one of the two, so likewise for the other. To this view went al-Tahawi. From al-Shafi'i (other view): he says 'I regret what I did'. Ahmad says: 'If I can, I will have intercourse with you.' The most correct is our view that verbal fay' when unable is a substitute for intercourse due to the saying of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud: 'The sick person makes fay' by his tongue', and they suffice as role models; and because the occurrence of divorce at expiry of the period is due to enmity and harm, which is removed by verbal fay' when unable for intercourse. Verbal fay' is conditional on two conditions in our school: one, that his saying 'I have returned' or what indicates it be within the ila' period; two, the continuity of inability from the time of ila' to the expiry of the period. Also, the existence of marriage at the time of verbal fay' is required; so if he made ila' while able, then later became unable due to illness or distance, or was unable when he made ila' and then became able during the period, verbal fay' is not valid; because it is a substitute for intercourse, so continuous inability throughout the period is required (contrary to Zufar). (Ayni, Fath) 'Fiatu ilayha': or nullify the ila' or return from it. (T) 'fi al-muddah': i.e., the ila' period, meaning he recovered (male or female). (T) 'fay'uhu bi al-wat'': i.e., in the vagina; if he has intercourse elsewhere, like the anus, it is not fay'. This is because he became able for the original before the substitute achieved the purpose, like the one who performs tayammum and then finds water before praying, so his tayammum is invalidated; similarly, verbal fay' is invalidated; because it is a substitute for intercourse. (Mukhtasar Fath)
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the concept of fay' (return) in ila'. It begins by defining fay' linguistically as 'return', citing the Quranic verse (2:226). It then distinguishes fay' into two types: original (intercourse) and substitute (verbal declaration). The main ruling is that fay' by the tongue (saying 'I have returned to her' or similar) is valid only when the husband is genuinely unable to perform intercourse due to the wife's illness, his illness, or similar real impediments. The passage clarifies that if the inability is legal (e.g., being in ihram), verbal fay' is not valid. The effects of verbal fay' are limited: it nullifies the ila' only in terms of preventing automatic divorce upon expiry of the period, but it does not nullify the oath itself; thus, if the husband later has intercourse (even after the period), he must pay expiation (kaffarah) for breaking the oath. Full fay' by intercourse nullifies both the divorce consequence and the oath. The passage then presents the Shafi'i objection, stating that fay' can only be by intercourse and that verbal fay' is invalid, with arguments. The Hanafi response relies on the hadith of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud that 'the sick person makes fay' by his tongue', and on the reasoning that the purpose of ila' rulings is to prevent harm, which is achieved by verbal return when intercourse is impossible. Finally, the passage details conditions for verbal fay': (1) the declaration must be within the ila' period, (2) the inability must be continuous from the start to the end of the period, and (3) the marriage must still exist at the time of declaration. It notes a disagreement with Zufar on the requirement of continuous inability. Then it discusses that if the husband becomes able before expiry, verbal fay' is invalidated, analogized to tayammum invalidated by finding water before prayer.

Key Terms
الفيء (al-fay'): Return, specifically the husband's return to marital relations after ila', either by intercourse or verbal declaration.
الحنث (al-hanth): Breaching an oath, here the ila' oath, requiring expiation (kaffarah).
خلف (khalaf): Substitute; verbal fay' is a substitute for intercourse when unable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of verbal fay' (saying 'I return to you') in ila'?
A) It completely nullifies the ila', including the oath.
B) It nullifies the ila' only regarding the divorce consequence, but the oath remains.
C) It has no effect; only intercourse counts as fay'.
D) It nullifies the oath immediately, requiring expiation.

Key Takeaway
Fay' in ila' has two forms: intercourse (original) and verbal declaration (substitute when unable); verbal fay' only prevents automatic divorce, not the oath.

Verbal Fay' in Ila': Conditions and the Shafi'i Objection
Conditions and dispute regarding verbal fay'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid verbal fay' according to the Hanafi school and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Verbal fay' requires continuous inability throughout the period and must be declared within that period.

This lesson delves into the detailed conditions that make verbal fay' valid in the Hanafi school and examines the Shafi'i objection that only intercourse can constitute fay'. The text provides the Hanafi response using a hadith and analogical reasoning. Understanding these conditions and the dispute is crucial for applying the ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i objection
The text presents the Shafi'i view: fay' can only be by intercourse. Their reasoning: the husband harms the wife by withholding intercourse, so fulfilling her right requires actual intercourse. Also, if verbal fay' were valid, it would constitute a breach (hanth) requiring immediate expiation.
This is the opposing position.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response relies on two proofs: (1) the hadith of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud: 'The sick person makes fay' by his tongue', which establishes the principle of verbal fay' in cases of inability. (2) Logical reasoning: the purpose of ila' rulings is to prevent harm (automatic divorce); verbal fay' achieves that purpose when intercourse is impossible.
This is the core evidence.
3
List the three conditions for verbal fay'
The text specifies: (1) The declaration must be within the ila' period. (2) The inability must be continuous from the time of ila' until the end of the period. (3) The marriage must exist at the time of declaration. Additionally, if the husband becomes able before the period ends, verbal fay' is invalidated.
Each condition must be memorized.
4
Understand the exception: Zufar's view
The text notes a disagreement with Zufar (a Hanafi jurist) who did not require continuous inability. The majority Hanafi view requires it, based on the analogy to tayammum: just as tayammum is invalidated by finding water before prayer, verbal fay' is invalidated by ability to have intercourse before the period ends.
This shows intra-madhhab variation.

(ط)] اعلم أن الفيء عبارة عن الرجوع يقال: "فاء الظل" إذا رجع، ولما قصد المولى باليمين منع حقها من الوطء سمي الرجوع عنه فيئا، قال تعالى: فَإِنْ فَاءُ والله (البقرة: ٢٢٦) أي رجعوا عن قصدهم، فالفيء في الإيلاء بمنزلة الرجعة في الطلاق. ثم الفيء على نوعين أصل وخلف، والأصل هو الجماع والخلف أن يقول بلسانه فئت إليها، وإنما يصح الفيء باللسان إذا عجز عن الأصل بسبب مرضها أو مرضه ونحوهما والمراد العجز حقيقة، فإن عجز حكماً لا حكم الإيلاء بأن كان محرما لا يكون فيؤه إلا بالوطء؛ لأنه لما كان قادراً حقيقة على الوطء لا يتحول الحكم إلى ما هو خلف عن الوطء. ثم إن قال ذلك أو ما دل عليه كراجعتك وارتجعتك سقط الإيلاء عندنا حتى لا يقع الطلاق بمضي المدة، لكن إذا وطئها بعد المدة أو قبلها بعد زوال الأعذار المذكورة فعليه الكفارة لتحقق الحنث به؛ لأن يمينه باقية في حق إن بطلت في حق الطلاق فالفيء باللسان يبطل الإيلاء في حق الطلاق فقط، والفيء بالوطء يبطله في حق الطلاق والحنث جميعاً، وقال الشافعي له: لا فيء إلا بالجماع، ولا يصح الفيء باللسان أصلا؛ لأنه ظلمها بمنع حقها وهو الوطء، فيكون إيفاؤه به؛ ولأنه لو كان فيئا لكان حنثا؛ لأن المعلق بالفيء حکمان وجوب الكفارة، وامتناع حكم الفرقة، والفيء باللسان لا يعتبر في حق أحد الحكمين، فكذا في حق حكم الآخر، وإليه ذهب الطحاوي، وعن الشافعي حه يقول: ندمت على ما فعلت. أحمد يقول: متى قدرت جامعتك. والأصح ما ما قلنا من أن الفيء باللسان عند العجز خلف عن الوطء لقول علي الله وابن مسعود ه: "يفيء المريض باللسان"، وكفى بهما قدوة؛ ولأن وقوع الطلاق عند مضي المدة باعتبار التعنت والإضرار بها، وذلك ينعدم بالفيء باللسان عند العجز عن الفيء بالجماع؛ لأن الفيء عبارة عن الرجوع، وذلك لا يوجد بهما إلا أن الفيء باللسان مشروط بشرطين عندنا أحدهما أن يكون قوله: فئت أو ما يدل عليه في مدة الإيلاء، والثاني: استمرار العجز من وقت الإيلاء إلى مضي مدة، واشترط أيضاً قيام النكاح وقت الفيء باللسان حتى لو آلى منها وهو قادر، ثم عجز الوطء بعد ذلك لمرض أو بعد مسافة ،وغيرهما، أو كان عاجزاً حين آلى وزال العجز في المدة لم يصح فيؤه باللسان؛ لأنه خلف عن الجماع، فيشترط فيه العجز المستوعب للمدة خلافاً لزفر. (ط) ففيؤه بالوطء أي في الفرج، فلو وطئ في غيره كدبر لا يكون فيئا، وإنما كان فيؤه بالوطء؛ لأنه قدر على الأصل قبل حصول المقصود بالخلف فصار كالمتيمم إذا قدر على الماء قبل أن يصلي، فيبطل التيمم فكذا الفيء باللسان يبطل؛ لأنه خلف عن الجماع. (مستخلص فتح
So his fay' is to say, etc. [i.e., the husband's return in these forms]. Know that fay' means return; it is said 'faa al-zill' when it returns. Since the husband intended by the oath to prevent her right to intercourse, the return from that is called fay'. Allah said 'fain fa'u' (2:226), meaning they returned from their intention. So fay' in ila' is like raj'a in divorce. Then fay' is of two types: original and substitute. The original is intercourse, and the substitute is to say with his tongue 'I have returned to her.' Verbal fay' is valid only if he is unable for the original due to her illness or his illness etc., and the intended is real inability. If there is a legal inability such as being in ihram, his only fay' is intercourse; because he is truly capable. Then if he says that or what indicates it like 'I take you back' or 'I have taken you back', the ila' is nullified according to us so that divorce does not occur upon expiry. However, if he has intercourse after the period or before it after the excuses disappear, he owes expiation due to the breaking of the oath; because his oath remains in that regard, though it is nullified regarding divorce. So verbal fay' nullifies ila' regarding divorce only, while intercourse nullifies it both regarding divorce and the breaking. Al-Shafi'i said: there is no fay' except by intercourse, and verbal fay' is not valid at all; because he harmed her by preventing her right of intercourse, so fulfillment is through it; and because if it were fay', it would be a breach (hanth) requiring expiation, since the rulings attached to fay' are two: obligation of expiation and prevention of separation, and verbal fay' is not considered for one of the two, so likewise for the other. To this view went al-Tahawi. From al-Shafi'i (other view): he says 'I regret what I did'. Ahmad says: 'If I can, I will have intercourse with you.' The most correct is our view that verbal fay' when unable is a substitute for intercourse due to the saying of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud: 'The sick person makes fay' by his tongue', and they suffice as role models; and because the occurrence of divorce at expiry of the period is due to enmity and harm, which is removed by verbal fay' when unable for intercourse. Verbal fay' is conditional on two conditions in our school: one, that his saying 'I have returned' or what indicates it be within the ila' period; two, the continuity of inability from the time of ila' to the expiry of the period. Also, the existence of marriage at the time of verbal fay' is required; so if he made ila' while able, then later became unable due to illness or distance, or was unable when he made ila' and then became able during the period, verbal fay' is not valid; because it is a substitute for intercourse, so continuous inability throughout the period is required (contrary to Zufar). (Ayni, Fath) 'Fiatu ilayha': or nullify the ila' or return from it. (T) 'fi al-muddah': i.e., the ila' period, meaning he recovered (male or female). (T) 'fay'uhu bi al-wat'': i.e., in the vagina; if he has intercourse elsewhere, like the anus, it is not fay'. This is because he became able for the original before the substitute achieved the purpose, like the one who performs tayammum and then finds water before praying, so his tayammum is invalidated; similarly, verbal fay' is invalidated; because it is a substitute for intercourse. (Mukhtasar Fath)

Parsing 'Anti 'Alayki Haram' by Intention
Categorization of the phrase by intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five possible legal effects of the phrase 'Anti 'alayki haram' by intention and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on dhihar.

Core Idea
Intention determines whether 'You are forbidden' is ila', dhihar, a lie, divorce, or triple divorce.

The phrase 'Anti 'alayki haram' (You are forbidden to me) is a kinayah that can produce multiple legal outcomes depending on what the husband intends. The Hanafi school provides a detailed parsing exercise: if he intends prohibition, it is ila'; if dhihar, it is dhihar (with dispute); if a lie, it is a lie; if divorce, it is a bain divorce; if three, it is triple divorce. Without intention, it defaults to ila' based on the Quranic verse that calls prohibiting the lawful an oath. Custom may also turn it into divorce without intention in later times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Determine what the phrase becomes if the husband intends nothing – it is ila' (oath) because prohibiting the lawful is an oath by Quranic text.
Quran 66:1-2
2
Categorize by each possible intention
For each explicit intention (dhihar, lie, divorce, three), state the legal effect as given in the matn.
The effect is tied to the intention of the husband.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on dhihar
Note that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow dhihar by intention while Muhammad rejects it due to missing the pillar of tashbih.
This dispute is recorded in al-Nawadir, not Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Examine the custom-based fatwa
Understand that in later practice, custom may cause the phrase to be treated as divorce without explicit intention, as mentioned in al-Fatawa.

(ع) إيلاء: إن نوى التحريم؛ لأن تحريم الحلال يمين كما قال تعالى : ولِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ لَكَ ﴾ (التحريم: ۱)، ثم قال: وقد فرض الله لكم تحلة أيمانكم الله فعلم أن تحريم الحلال يمين، وقوله: "أو لم ينو شيئاً" أي لا ظهاراً ولا طلاقاً ولا إيلاء ولا كذباً. (فتح) أو لم ينو شيئاً : لأن تحريم الحلال يمين بالنص. And 'You are forbidden to me' is ila' if he intends prohibition, or if he intends nothing; and it is dhihar if he intends dhihar; and it is a lie if he intends lying; and a bain divorce if he intends divorce; and three if he intends three. And in al-Fatawa: if he says to his wife 'You are forbidden to me', and 'forbidden' he intends divorce but did not intend divorce, then it is according to the faces. (Ayni) Ila': if he intends prohibition, because prohibiting the lawful is an oath, as Allah said 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful to you?' (66:1), then He said 'Allah has ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths', so it is known that prohibiting the lawful is an oath. And his saying 'or if he intends nothing' means not dhihar, not divorce, not ila', nor a lie. (Fath) 'Or if he intends nothing': because prohibiting the lawful is an oath by textual proof. (Ayni) 'And dhihar if he intends it' i.e., dhihar. This according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, because it contains prohibition, so if he intends it, it is valid because it can bear it. According to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, it is not dhihar because it lacks its pillar, which is comparing a lawful woman to a forbidden one [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:275]. And in al-Hidayah the disagreement of Muhammad is mentioned but not in Zahir al-Riwayah; it is transmitted by al-Sarakhsi in al-Nawadir, and what is mentioned in al-Jami' from Muhammad is like the two of them, and Jami' al-Fiqh is the work of Abu Yusuf. (Fath) 'If he intends lying' [because he intended the literal meaning of his words, so it is a lie in reality (Ayni)]; with fatha on the kaf and kasra on the mu'jama, and it is permissible with kasra and sukun on the dhal. It is said: he is not believed judicially because it is an oath outwardly, so he is not believed in diverting it to something else. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:275] 'Raj'iyyah? ...' 'If he intends divorce because it is from the kinayat words, whether he intends no number or less than three, and whether he intends bain or ... His saying: 'and three if he intends it'; because 'anti' 'alayki haram' is from the kinayat, and in them intention of three is valid. And it is said: prohibition is turned to divorce without intention due to custom, especially in our time, and the author pointed to that by his saying 'and in al-Fatawa etc.' (Ayni, Fath) 'And three' i.e., the fifth is that it is three divorces. (Ayni) 'If he intends it' i.e., three, and it is included in the kinayat. (Ayni) 'Al-Fatawa': thus it occurs in many copies, and in some copies 'and in al-Fatwa' i.e., that which the mufti gives as fatwa, and that is preferable. (Fath) It ends.

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'Anti 'alayki haram' (You are forbidden to me). The ruling is that the legal effect depends on the husband's intention. If he intends tahrim (prohibition of intercourse), it is ila' because prohibiting the lawful is an oath, citing the Quranic verse (66:1-2). If he intends no specific category, it defaults to ila'. If he intends dhihar, it is dhihar according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad disagrees because the phrase lacks the explicit comparison (tashbih) of a wife to a mahram. If he intends to lie, it is a lie but judicially it is treated as an oath. If he intends divorce, it is a bain divorce; if he intends three, it is triple divorce. The passage also notes that due to custom, especially in later times, the phrase may be interpreted as divorce without explicit intention, as indicated in al-Fatawa. The text includes references to the opinions of the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad, and notes that the view of Muhammad is recorded in al-Nawadir, not in Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage ends by indicating that the fatwa in many copies refers to the mufti's preferred view.

Key Terms
الحرام (al-haram): Forbidden; when used as a predicate (e.g., 'you are forbidden to me'), it becomes a kinayah requiring intention.
الكنايات (al-kinayat): Allusive words that require intention to produce a specific legal effect (e.g., divorce).
الظهار (al-zihar): A husband's statement comparing his wife to a mahram, requiring expiation before intercourse.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'Anti 'alayki haram' is a kinayah whose legal effect is determined by the husband's intention, with ila' as the default; intention can also make it dhihar, a lie, bain divorce, or triple divorce.

Legal Effects of Saying 'Anti 'Alayya Haram' to One's Wife
Ruling with proof: five outcomes based on intention and custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the Hanafi ruling for the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram' based on the husband's intention and custom, and distinguish the Hanafi position from other schools.

Core Idea
Making lawful unlawful is an oath; the phrase's effect depends on intention and custom can override intention.

This lesson unpacks the legal consequences of a husband saying 'you are forbidden to me' to his wife. The Hanafi school, grounded in Quran 66:1-2, treats such a statement as an oath unless intention or custom dictates otherwise. Understanding the five possible outcomes based on intention, and the critical role of custom, is essential for correctly applying this ruling in real-life scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the utterance 'anti 'alayya haram' by a husband to his wife. The primary ruling is that it is an oath (yamin), as per Quran 66:1-2, which requires expiation if violated.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is Quran 66:1-2, where Allah rebukes the Prophet for making lawful unlawful and immediately mentions expiation of oaths, establishing that such statements are oaths.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful to you?' followed by 'Allah has ordained for you the expiation of your oaths' directly links making lawful unlawful to an oath requiring expiation.
4
Map the five outcomes by intention
The text lists five possibilities based on what the husband intends: i) ila' (vow to abstain) if he intends prohibition; ii) zihar (injurious comparison) if he intends that (according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf); iii) falsehood if he intends to lie; iv) one bain divorce if he intends divorce; v) three divorces if he intends triple divorce. If he intends nothing, it remains an oath.
Muhammad al-Shaybani differs on zihar, requiring explicit comparison.
5
Evaluate the role of custom and the mufta bihi
If in a given society the word 'haram' is commonly understood to mean divorce, the Hanafi fatwa rules that divorce occurs even without intention, as per the principle that 'urf (custom) is given weight. This is the mufta bihi position.
The author cites 'al-Fatawa' as the basis for this reliance on custom.

(ع)
Book of Divorce. Chapter of Ila'. She returned to him, and if he is able during the period then his fay' (resumption) is by intercourse. And 'you are forbidden to me' is ila' if he intends prohibition. The one swearing on intercourse. So the first is ila'. Or he did not intend anything, and it is zihar if he intends it, and falsehood if he intends falsehood, and bainuna if he intends divorce. So the second is zihar. Zihar: so the third is null. So the fourth is one bain divorce. And three if he intends it. In al-Fatawa: if he says to his wife 'you are forbidden to me' and the forbidden in his view means divorce but he did not intend divorce. The time of ila' extends to the end of the period, and it is also stipulated that marriage remain at the time of fay' by tongue. So if he swears ila' from her while able, then later becomes unable for intercourse due to illness or distance or other, or was unable at the time of ila' and the inability ends within the period, his fay' is not valid by tongue because it is a substitute for intercourse, so continuous inability for the entire period is required, contrary to Zufar. (Ayni, Fath) 'Fata ilayha': She annulled the ila' or returned from it. (T) 'Fi al-mudda': The period of ila'. (T) 'Fafay'uhu bil-wati': In the vagina. Fornication in other like anus is not fay'. His fay' is by intercourse because he is able to do the original before the purpose is achieved by the substitute, like the one performing tayammum when he finds water before praying – tayammum is invalidated. Similarly, fay' by tongue is invalidated because it is a substitute for intercourse. (Mukhtasar Fath) 'Wa anti': If he says to his wife 'you are forbidden' etc., it has several aspects. (A) Ila': if he intends prohibition because making lawful unlawful is an oath as Allah said: 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful to you?' (al-Tahrim:1), then He said: 'Allah has ordained for you the expiation of your oaths' so it is known that making lawful unlawful is an oath. And his saying 'or he did not intend anything' means not zihar, not divorce, not ila', not falsehood. (Fath) 'Or he did not intend anything': because making lawful unlawful is an oath by the text. (A) And zihar if he intends it – this with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, because it contains prohibition, so if he intends it, it is valid because it can bear it. With Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, it is not zihar because its pillar is missing, which is comparing a permissible woman to a prohibited one. It is mentioned in al-Hidayah that Muhammad differed, but it is not mentioned in the Zahir al-Riwayah; rather al-Sarakhsi narrated it from al-Nawadir. What is mentioned in Jami' from Muhammad is like their view. (Fath) If he intends falsehood: because he intended the literal meaning of his words so it is literally falsehood. (A) It is said: he is not believed judicially because it is an oath externally, so he is not believed in turning it to something else. (Ramz al-Haqaiq) Revocable. If he intends divorce because it is from the kinayah (ambiguous) words, whether he did not intend a number or intended less than three, and whether he intended bain or revocable. And his saying 'and three if he intends it': because 'anti' 'alayya' 'haram' are kinayah words, and intending three is valid in them. It is said: the prohibition is turned to divorce without intention due to custom, especially in our time. The author indicated this by his saying 'in al-Fatawa etc.' (Ayni, Fath) 'Wa thalath': the fifth is three divorces. (A) 'In nawahu': meaning three, and it is valid in kinayah. (A) 'al-Fatawa': occurs in many copies; in some copies it occurs as 'al-fatwa' meaning the fatwa that is given, and it is the preferred. (Fath) 'Waqa'a al-talaq ilkh': meaning the bain divorce, and he did not restrict it to it, relying on what he previously stated that this is the ruling of every kinayah and he counted it among them. (Fath) Meaning if he says to his wife 'you are forbidden to me' while the forbidden in his view is divorce, divorce falls on his wife without intention. The fatwa is upon this due to prevalent custom. Similarly his saying 'everything halal is forbidden to me'. For this reason only men swear by it, so if a woman swears, she is liable for expiation of an oath upon violation, and if he had no wife, it is an oath. From this they said: if he intends other than that, he is not believed judicially. The school of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, is that if he intends divorce or zihar, it is as he intended; if he intends an oath or did not intend anything, he must pay expiation of an oath. According to Malik: 'you are forbidden to me' is three divorces for a consummated wife, whether he intended or not, and one for an unconsummated one. According to Ahmad: it is zihar whether he intended or not; from him also: it is an oath absolutely; from him also: divorce. And if he says: 'my wife is forbidden to me' and he has four wives, it falls on each one as one bain divorce. Some say: only one of them divorces, and this explanation is the most apparent and appropriate. (Ramz al-Haqaiq) (Mukhtasar, Fath) 'Urfan': meaning consideration of custom because of the general usage of people: 'you are forbidden to me' refers to divorce. (A)
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with its commentaries, explains the legal effects of a husband saying 'you are forbidden to me' (anti 'alayya haram) to his wife. The primary ruling is that this phrase constitutes ila' (a vow to abstain from intercourse) if he intends prohibition. The foundation is Quran 66:1-2, which treats making lawful unlawful as an oath, requiring expiation. The text then enumerates five possible outcomes based on intention: 1) ila' if he intends prohibition; 2) zihar (injurious comparison) if he intends that, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (though Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, considering zihar requires explicit comparison); 3) falsehood if he intends to lie; 4) one bain divorce if he intends divorce; 5) three divorces if he intends triple divorce. The passage notes that if he intends nothing, it remains a simple oath. A crucial practical point: if the husband considers 'haram' to mean divorce, then divorce occurs even without intention, due to prevailing custom; this is the position relied upon for fatwa. The text also clarifies the condition for valid fay' in ila': it must be actual intercourse, not merely verbal, because fay' is the substitute for intercourse, analogous to tayammum being invalidated by finding water. The passage concludes with a comparative overview: Shafi'i treats it based on intention; Malik imposes triple divorce for a consummated wife regardless of intention; and Ahmad has multiple narrations (zihar, oath, or divorce). The Hanafi position balances the literal wording with custom and intention.

Key Terms
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): A vow by a husband to abstain from sexual intercourse with his wife for a period of four months or more, after which he must either resume (fay') or divorce.
الفيء (al-fay'): The resumption of sexual intercourse within the period of ila'; without it, the husband may be forced to divorce.
كناية (kināyah): An ambiguous word that can imply divorce when intended; here, 'anti 'alayya haram' is kinayah if it customarily means divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says 'anti 'alayya haram' intending nothing at all, what is the ruling?
A) It is ila' (a vow to abstain).
B) It is zihar (injurious comparison).
C) It is an oath requiring expiation.
D) It is a bain divorce.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'anti 'alayya haram' in Hanafi law is an oath whose effect depends on intention and custom; when custom equates it with divorce, it becomes divorce without intention.

The Hanafi Ruling on 'Anti Alayya Haram'
States Hanafi ruling and disputes on saying 'anti alayya haram'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'anti alayya haram', explain the role of custom, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali positions.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, 'anti alayya haram' results in divorce if custom so dictates, even without intention.

When a husband says to his wife 'anti alayya haram' (you are forbidden to me), what legal effect does it have? The answer varies across madhhabs. This lesson focuses on the Hanafi position, which relies heavily on prevalent custom ('urf) to determine the meaning. We will also explore the differences with other schools and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the core ruling: if the husband says 'anti alayya haram' and the custom is to use this phrase for divorce, divorce occurs without need for intention. The fatwa is upon this based on dominant custom.
The text says 'وعليه الفتوى' indicating the relied-upon position.
2
Examine the basis: custom ('urf)
Understand that Hanafi fiqh gives weight to 'urf in interpreting ambiguous expressions (kinayah). Custom specifies the meaning of 'haram' in this context.
This is because the general public uses the phrase to mean divorce.
3
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that intention determines the effect: if he intended divorce or zihar, it is as intended; otherwise it is an oath requiring kaffarah. The Hanafi response is that custom removes the need for explicit intention.
Thamarah al-khilaf: in Shafi'i, if no intention of divorce, no divorce; in Hanafi, divorce occurs due to custom.
4
Note the Maliki and Hanbali positions
Maliki: three divorces in consummated marriage, one in non-consummated, regardless of intention. Hanbali: a riwayah states it is zihar, another that it is an oath, another that it is divorce. The Hanafi school diverges on multiple details.
This shows the breadth of ikhtilaf on this single expression.
5
Apply the ancillary ruling for multiple wives
If a man says 'my wife is forbidden to me' and has four wives, the preferred view is that one irrevocable divorce falls on each wife. This shows how the ruling is applied in practice.
The text mentions an alternative view that only one wife is divorced, but the chosen view is the most apparent.

Source Text

وقع الطلاق، وجعل ناوياً عرفاً.
وقع الطلاق إلخ : [ أي البائن ولم يقيده به اكتفاء بما قدمه من أ أن هذا حكم كل كناية وقد عدّه منها. (فتح)] أي إذا قال لامرأته: أنت علي حرام والحال أن الحرام عنده طلاق يقع الطلاق على امرأته من غير نية، وعليه الفتوى لغالب العرف، وكذا قوله: كل حلال علي حرام ولهذا لا يحلف به إلا الرجال حتى لو حلف المرأة لزمها بالحنث كفارة يمين ولو لم يكن له امرأة كان يمينا وعن هذا قالوا لو نوى غيره لا يصدق قضاء، ومذهب الشافعي ليه في هذا أنه لو نوى طلاقاً أو ظهاراً فكما نوى، ولو نوى يميناً أو لم ينو شيئاً فعليه كفارة يمين. وعند مالك: أنت على حرام طلقات ثلاث في الموطوءة نواه أو لا، وواحدة في غيرها، وعند أحمد: ظهار نواه أم لا، وعنه : يمين مطلقاً، مطلقاً، وعنه : طلاق ولو قال: امرأتي علي حرام وله أربع نسوة يقع على كل واحدة تطليقة بائنة، وقيل: تطلق واحدة منهن، وإليه البيان وهو الأظهر والأشبة رمز الحقائق: ٢٧٥/١] (مستخلص، فتح) عرفاً: أي اعتباراً للعرف لإطلاق الناس جملة، أنت علي حرام على الطلاق. (ع)
Book of Divorce. Chapter of Ila'. Divorce occurs, and he is considered as intending according to custom. 'Divorce occurs' etc.: i.e., the irrevocable divorce, and he did not restrict it to that, relying on what he presented that this is the ruling of every kinayah and he counted it among them. (Fath) That is, if he says to his wife: 'You are forbidden to me,' and the state is that 'forbidden' according to him is divorce, divorce occurs upon his wife without intention, and the fatwa is upon this due to prevalent custom. Similarly his saying: 'Everything lawful is forbidden to me.' For this reason, only men swear by it, so if a woman swears, upon breaking the oath she owes the expiatory offering of an oath. And if he had no wife, it is an oath. For this reason they said: if he intended other than divorce, he is not believed in judicial proceedings. The madhhab of al-Shafi'i is that if he intended divorce or zihar, it is as he intended; if he intended an oath or did not intend anything, he owes the expiatory offering of an oath. According to Malik: 'You are forbidden to me' is three divorces in the case of a consummated marriage, whether he intended or not, and one in other cases. According to Ahmad: it is zihar whether he intended or not; and from him: it is an oath absolutely; and from him: it is divorce. And if he said: 'My wife is forbidden to me' and he has four wives, one irrevocable divorce falls upon each wife. It is said: one of them is divorced, and the explanation is to that, and it is the most apparent and most appropriate. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/275) (Mukhtalas, Fath) Customarily: i.e., considering custom due to the general usage of people saying 'You are forbidden to me' for divorce. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from the chapter of Ila' deals with the ruling when a husband says to his wife 'anti alayya haram' (you are forbidden to me). The author first states the Hanafi position: divorce occurs without need for intention if the custom is to use this phrase for divorce, and the fatwa is based on prevalent custom. This is followed by a comparison with other madhhabs. The text then discusses an ancillary ruling: if a man has four wives and says 'my wife is forbidden to me', according to the preferred view, one irrevocable divorce falls upon each wife. The passage ends by emphasizing that custom is considered because people commonly use this phrase to mean divorce.

Key Terms
حرام (haram): In this context, when a husband says 'anti alayya haram', it is interpreted according to custom; it may effect divorce, zihar, or oath depending on intention and madhhab.
عرف (urf): Custom; the prevalent practice of people in using a phrase. In this ruling, 'urf determines that 'anti alayya haram' means divorce.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that can imply divorce but requires intention or custom to make it effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because custom determines the meaning.
B) No, intention is always required.
C) Yes, but only if he meant zihar.
D) No, it is always an oath.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on prevalent custom to determine the legal effect of 'anti alayya haram', making divorce effective without intention if custom so dictates.
